"You look terrible," Grim said.

"Not a joke moment."

"Then why are you here?"

Lars staggered to the distributor and threaded the lower cable around its frame. The surface was hot through his gloves. Not burning. Not quite. The chamber smell thickened. His suit alarm flashed yellow, then orange.

Valdrun watched him closely.

"It affects you too."

"Everything affects me. I'm sensitive."

"Lars."

The name landed differently from him.

Gentler.

Older.

Lars tightened the final lock and stepped back.

"Pull slowly," he said.

Grim relayed the command. The work robot outside the field engaged.

The cable tightened.

The overhead beam groaned.

"Stop," Lars said.

The pull stopped.

He recalculated. The first angle would drag the distributor sideways across Valdrun's pinned actuator column and probably tear something important. He needed lift first, then translation.

He moved to the left, attached a secondary guide line, and nearly fell.

Grim took one step into the chamber.

"Stay back," Lars snapped.

Grim froze.

"I'm fine."

"You are swaying."

"That's tactical."

"It is not."

Lars wrapped the guide line around a floor anchor that surfaced when he kicked the right panel. Again, the station obeyed him before he understood why.

"Now," he said. "Ten centimeters lift. No more."

The robot pulled.

The distributor rose with a sound like a mountain changing its mind.

Valdrun inhaled sharply.

"Hold," Lars said.

The cable held.

"Guide line left. Slow."

Grim controlled the secondary winch. The distributor shifted away from Valdrun's torso by one centimeter, then three, then six.

The overhead beam screamed.

"Stop."

Lars stumbled forward, grabbed Valdrun under one arm, and pulled.

Valdrun did not move.

"You weigh a lot."

"Rude."

"Accurate."

Valdrun's free hand dug into the floor. His damaged side sparked once, then locked. Lars pulled again. Something in his own shoulders burned. Not muscle. Deeper. As if the body had reserves he had never been shown and was angry about being asked.

Valdrun slid half a meter.

"Again," Lars gasped.

The distributor shifted.

Valdrun pushed.

Lars pulled.

They came free together.

"Release load," Lars said.

The work robot eased the distributor down. It struck the floor with a deep metallic boom.

For one second, everything held.

Then Lars's vision folded inward.

He heard Grim shout his name.

He heard Valdrun say something in a language that made the locked places in him blaze.

He heard Ödesvaka report medical emergency with obscene calm.

Then the chamber, the station, and the impossible blue light went away.

Chapter 5: The Helmet

Something beeped.

Lars disliked it immediately.

Beep.

Beep.

Beep.

The sound had the personality of hospital equipment: smug, repetitive, convinced of its own importance. He tried to open his eyes and discovered someone had replaced reality with a blurred white rectangle.

Voices moved around him.

One was Grim.

One was deeper, rougher, unfamiliar and familiar at the same time.

One was a machine that sounded like it would report a fire in the same tone it used to announce soup.

Lars closed his eyes again.

This proved to be a mistake because the beeping remained.

The second time he woke, the world had improved enough to include edges.

He lay in a medical bed in a white room with black equipment columns and pale blue diagnostic layers moving silently above him. The walls were smooth, rounded, and much too clean. No stains. No cracked paneling. No old Rim smell. The air was perfect. Lars distrusted perfect air.

Grim sat beside the bed holding a cup of coffee.

Not drinking it.

Holding it like a threat.

"If that is for me," Lars said, "you are forgiven for whatever expression you are making."

Grim's head tilted. "You cannot see my expression."

"I can hear it."

"You collapsed."

"People do that."

"For forty-eight hours?"

Lars blinked.

"That seems excessive."

"We had to keep you sedated," said the rough voice.

Valdrun stood near the foot of the bed.

Free, now. Tall. Very tall. Blue-pale skin, long limbs, broad shoulders, and visible integrated structure along his neck and left side where biological tissue met engineered material without apology. Not a robot. Not a man in the ordinary sense. Something half human, half machine, and entirely comfortable being terrifying in a quiet room.

He looked like a Viking who had survived a starship factory accident.

"You," Lars said.

"Me."

"You were under a power thing."

"Power distributor."

"I rescued you."

"You did."

"Good."

Valdrun smiled.

Grim handed Lars the coffee.

Lars took it with both hands. His fingers trembled slightly. He pretended they did not.

"What hit me?" he asked.

"Radiation, chemical contamination, local field interference, and your own nanomedical systems attempting to reconcile a neurological lock with emergency repair demands," Valdrun said.

Lars lowered the cup.

"My what?"

Pain began at the base of his neck.

Not a warning this time.

A guillotine being raised.

Grim leaned forward. "Careful."

Valdrun nodded once. "Right. We do not push through the lock verbally."

"I would appreciate everyone not discussing my body like it has unauthorized aftermarket parts."

Grim and Valdrun exchanged a look.

Lars pointed at them.

"No meaningful looks."

"We are in a room full of meaning."

"Then look at the wall."

Ödesvaka floated near the ceiling, half in shadow, symbols moving across its spherical body.

"Captain medical stability at seventy-two percent," the drone announced.

"Do not call me captain."

"Command title suppressed."

"Thank you."

"Suppression logged."

"Everything is logged with you."

"Correct."

Lars drank coffee and tried to assemble the last clear memory.

Then nothing.

"Why did the chamber affect Grim faster than me?" Lars asked.

Grim answered before Valdrun could. "The field damaged synthetic control loops and non-biological quantum substrates. My biological core tolerated the edge, but my motor coordination started desynchronizing. Deeper in, I would likely have become modern sculpture."

"Bad sculpture."

"Probably."

Valdrun sat in the chair opposite Grim. The chair shaped itself to him. Lars noticed that and tried not to think about how natural it looked.

"The accident began forty-nine years ago," Valdrun said. "A distributor failure in the auxiliary chamber. It should have been routine. A local mechanical collapse, toxic outgassing from damaged insulation layers, and a field harmonic from the power grid that disabled maintenance robotics. I was able to prevent cascade failure, but the distributor pinned me before I could clear the chamber."

"Forty-nine years under a machine."

"Yes."

"That sounds boring."

"Deeply."

"Why didn't you signal someone?"

"I did. Locally. The station could not risk external transmission under watch-state. Too many hostile receivers near the galactic rim. Radio would spread and die after a few light-years. Tightbeam paths were blocked or unsafe. The only vessel in this facility able to cross the required distance quietly was locked under your command envelope."

"My command envelope."

"Yes."

Pain pressed.

Lars took more coffee.

"Convenient."

"Inconvenient for me."

"Fair."

Valdrun leaned forward. "Your arrival was not random."

Lars raised a hand. "If you are about to say destiny, I will throw this very expensive coffee at you."

"Good. Destiny is a lazy word."

Lars lowered his hand slightly.

"Continue."

"There are systems that can bias probability without controlling choice. Signals too weak to be orders. Pattern nudges. Cognitive resonance through nanoscale communication, memory locks, old navigation habits, stress responses. You felt urgency because parts of you had enough information to know where to go, but not enough permission to explain why."

That was better.

Still horrible.

But better.

"So not fate," Lars said.

"No. Engineering."

"I hate when engineering does psychology."

"It hates you back," Grim said.

Lars looked between them. "And the headache?"

Valdrun's expression softened.

"A safeguard. You were not meant to think directly about certain memories until you returned to a proper node."

"Hypnosis."

The word came from Lars's own mouth.

The room froze.

The pain rose, then stopped.

Not gone.

Paused.

Valdrun watched him carefully.

"Yes."

Lars expected the migraine to crush him.

It did not.

Instead a thin line of memory opened: a room like this one, but larger; seven adults standing in a circle; a woman with dark hair and star-bright eyes; someone laughing because the plan was both brilliant and appalling.

Then the line closed.

Lars exhaled slowly.

"I don't want the whole explanation yet."

"Wise," Valdrun said.

"I want the practical version."

Valdrun stood and crossed to a device in the center of the medical room. It had been folded into the floor when Lars first woke. Now it rose in three clean arcs: a chair, a halo-like frame, and a set of optical emitters that glowed in wavelengths that made Grim adjust his eyes again.

"This can remove the lock in stages," Valdrun said. "Not all at once. Your body just survived a bad interaction between the lock and emergency repair systems. If we leave it as is, the next shock could kill you or scramble memory access worse than death."

"Worse than death is a strong sales pitch."

"You have died before."

Silence.

Grim stopped moving.

Lars looked at Valdrun.

"That was not the practical version."

"No," Valdrun said. "But it is the part that explains why I am not asking."

The room seemed too bright.

Lars's hands tightened around the coffee cup until the material warned him with a soft tone.

"How many times?"

"Not now."

"You just dropped dead-before on me and now you're rationing?"

"Yes."

Lars almost laughed. It came out wrong.

Grim spoke quietly. "Lars."

"I know."

He swung his legs over the side of the bed. The room tilted. He waited until it stopped.

"If this chair turns me into a religious person, I am blaming both of you."

"It will not," Valdrun said.

"If I start speaking in riddles?"

"We sedate you."

"Good protocol."

He stood, took one step, and nearly sat down by accident. Grim moved to help, then stopped when Lars glared.

"I am fine."

"You are seventy-two percent stable," Ödesvaka said.

"No one asked the ball."

"Logged."

Valdrun helped him into the chair without making it look like help, which Lars appreciated and resented. The material adjusted around his back. The halo lowered over his head but did not touch him. Pale blue patterns formed in the air, layered with ultraviolet structures Grim could barely track and Lars could see too well.

"Look at the center," Valdrun said. "Do not chase memories. Let the system map the lock first."

"How does it work?"

"Neural phase alignment, memory index verification, controlled suggestion reversal, nanomedical synchronization."

"That sounded almost respectable."

"It is respectable."

"Then why does it look like a dentist chair designed by a cult?"

Grim said, "Because you are frightened."

Lars looked at him.

Grim did not soften it.

For once, Lars did not answer.

The pattern began to move.

At first it was only light. Then timing. Then pressure. The emitters pulsed in ratios that did not repeat but never became random. His breathing tried to follow them. His pulse corrected. Somewhere in his blood, systems he did not remember owning answered.

The headache changed shape.

It had been a wall.

Now it became a door.

The first thing through was not grand.

Not Lyra.

Not war.

Not ancient secrets.

It was a smell.

Fresh bread in a station corridor.

Someone's hand ruffling his hair.

A voice: "Laurðinn, if you rewire the nursery lights again, at least make them stop blinking before Valdrun sees."

Then another memory slammed into place.

He was small, running along a white corridor on Station Zero.

He was not from Stockholm.

He had never been from Stockholm.

His eyes opened.

The medical room returned.

Valdrun stood beside the chair, face grave.

Grim's metal hands were clenched.

Ödesvaka hovered motionless.

Lars tried to speak.

For once, no joke arrived first.

"Station Zero," he said.

Valdrun nodded.

"Your birthplace."

Lars looked at his hands.

They were still his hands.

That seemed unlikely.

"How old am I?"

Valdrun did not look away.

"Six hundred and forty-one."

The answer passed through Lars like cold water.

Somewhere far behind him, a fashionable idiot with yellow sunglasses and too much money fell silent.

The man in the chair remained.

He took one breath.

Then another.

"I need," Lars said slowly, "a much stronger coffee."

Grim made a sound that was almost relief.

Valdrun smiled.

"That, at least, I remember."

Chapter 6: Stronger Coffee

The stronger coffee arrived in a cup that looked too elegant for what it contained.

Lars took one careful sip and immediately forgave the cup.

"That," he said, "could restart a dead moon."

"It has restarted you before," Valdrun said.

Lars held up one finger. "We are limiting the amount of before in this conversation."

"Reasonable."

"Also the amount of dead."

"Less reasonable, but noted."

Grim stood near the medical room's wall display with four diagnostic layers open in front of him. Station Zero had given him access to Lars's live medical telemetry after Valdrun authorized it and Lars complained for six straight minutes. Grim had then spent three more minutes complaining about the interface being too good.

The display showed Lars as a translucent figure surrounded by cellular activity, neural synchronization bands, blood chemistry maps, and several layers Grim had labeled unacceptable weirdness.

"I can see that label," Lars said.

"It is accurate."

"Medical terminology has collapsed since I was thirty."

Valdrun folded his arms. "You were never thirty."

Lars looked at him.

Valdrun sighed. "You believed you were around thirty."

"Your current cover identity was designed to pass casual scrutiny. Birth records, education fragments, social media history, financial trails, medical records. Stockholm was useful because your surface accent and habits already leaned Swedish."

"Surface accent?"

"You were raised with several languages here. Galactic Swedish became dominant later, for obvious historical reasons."

"I remember Stockholm."

"You remember inserted connective tissue. Not all of it is false. Some is drawn from actual stays on Earth and in Swedish habitats. The lock did not simply erase and replace. It compressed, braided, and redirected."

Lars drank coffee.

That was better than answering.

The medical chair had given him one clean fact and a thousand dirty implications. Six hundred and forty-one. Born on Station Zero. Not from Stockholm. His name had been Laurðinn before it became Lars Odin, which sounded like someone had taken an old thing and filed the dangerous edges off.

He did not feel six hundred and forty-one.

He felt tired, hungry, irritated, and possibly under-caffeinated.

Apparently some truths lacked theatrical ambition.

"How much is still locked?" Grim asked.

Valdrun looked to the diagnostic display. "Most of the autobiographical chain. The first stage restored identity anchors: birthplace, age, primary name recognition, station familiarity, and enough medical access to stop his own systems fighting the lock."

"So I know who I am," Lars said, "but not what the idiot did."

"A fair summary."

Valdrun crossed to the wall and opened a new display. It showed Station Zero in layered geometry: hangars, medical rings, habitat districts, manufacturing zones, archives, power loops, hidden docks, and the damaged auxiliary chamber now marked amber instead of red.

"This place," Valdrun said, "is Arkology Zero. Station Zero is the old public translation. It sits a few light-years into the KBC Void, but not in ordinary space the way The Rim does. The outer approach you saw was only the coupling surface. The real structure is folded inside a spacetime bubble."

"That explains the rude windows."

"It explains why external observers have never mapped us, why conventional signals fail, and why only certain ships can enter without tearing themselves into interesting vapor."

Lars looked at the map.

It was too large. Not city-large. Civilization-large. Not in population now, maybe, but in intent. A place built by people who expected time to be an engineering parameter.

"How many are here?"

Valdrun's face changed.

Not much.

Enough.

"Awake? Now? Me, Ödesvaka, the station intelligences, and some limited biological cultures in preservation. You and Grim arrived from outside. Most residential sectors are empty or in conservation."

"Empty because everyone left?"

"Because everyone hid."

The word hid did not trigger the old migraine.

That almost made it worse. The lock had stopped punishing him, which meant the words could get all the way in.

Lars set down the coffee.

"Practical version."

Valdrun nodded. "In the 2180s, Lyra initiated Alpha Fourteen. The goal was survival. Not conquest, not retreat from responsibility, not some dramatic moral purity. Survival. Our numbers were too small, our technology too desirable, and the galaxy had begun doing what the galaxy always does when someone finds a lever: every tyrant, oligarch, sect, and military cult wanted to pull it."

"So you made people forget."

"We made ourselves forget. Everyone except seven guardians remained under continuity exemption. The others were dispersed into ordinary civilization with layered identities and hidden triggers. They could live, work, adapt, and avoid exposing what they were."

"What we were."

"Yes."

The word landed.

Not painfully.

He let it remain.

"The seven guardians stayed awake," Valdrun continued. "Not rulers. Keys. Maintenance of the hidden nodes, dehypnotization systems, memory restoration, emergency activation. We delegated constantly. Moved constantly. Avoided central command."

"Who?"

Valdrun hesitated.

Lars leaned back. "If that hesitation is for my health, I dislike it but approve."

"It is. Names carry memory weight. Some will be easier after the next stage."

"Give me the ones I already stepped in."

"Lyra Vargstjärna."

The name did not hurt.

It rang instead.

Far away.

"Founder," Valdrun said. "First carrier of the Pluto nanobot line."

Grim's lenses brightened, but he did not interrupt.

"Your mother and father," Valdrun continued. "Also guardians. Their names are not for this stage."

Lars's fingers curled around the cup.

"Alive?"

"Unknown."

The word was clean and brutal.

Lars nodded once.

"You," he said.

"Me."

"Vigor."

Valdrun's eyebrow lifted.

Lars felt, absurdly, pleased with himself.

"He wore a false beard," Lars said. "No real farmer with that much defensive infrastructure gives away Xaltor X without knowing exactly who is sitting at his table."

"Vigor Hallman is one of the seven."

"I hugged a guardian while carrying strategic groceries."

"That sounds like him."

"And two more?"

"Later."

"Fine."

Grim enlarged part of the station map. "Why did Vigor not come here if he is awake?"

"He likely did not know I was trapped. Station Zero's external transmission policy under watch-state is extremely restrictive. After the chamber accident, Ödesvaka could not authorize a broad signal without my command, and I could not reach a command surface. Havets Sista Sång was under Lars's old command envelope."

"That is bad redundancy," Lars said.

Valdrun's mouth tightened. "Yes."

"Not mildly bad. Historically embarrassing bad."

"Yes."

"Good. As long as we agree."

"We have had forty-nine years to agree."

Lars stood too quickly, regretted it, and pretended not to. The medical display complained. Grim looked ready to do something helpful and therefore irritating.

"So," Lars said, "Black Hole B-7 attacks me on The Rim. Vigor gives me poison whisky and beef. One word from Grim's old fragments nearly splits my skull. I leave for the Void. We find Station Zero. I unlock an invisible ship. We rescue you from a forty-nine-year engineering scandal. I learn I am six hundred and forty-one, not Swedish in the way I thought, and possibly full of unauthorized machines."

"Mostly correct," Grim said.

"Mostly?"

"You are still Swedish in several historically meaningful ways."

"And your machines are probably authorized by you."

"I hate that more."

Valdrun watched him with an expression Lars did not have a category for. Grief, maybe. Relief. Amusement. The tired affection of someone who had known him long enough to be unsurprised by his worst habits.

That was one of the hardest parts.

Being known by a stranger.

"Why now?" Lars asked. "Not destiny. Not poetry. Why did the pressure spike now?"

Valdrun gestured to the wall. The station replaced the map with a slow-moving model of the Milky Way: traffic corridors, conflict zones, collapsed colonies, energy consumption clusters, signal noise, corporate sovereignties, war fronts, quarantine regions, slave routes hidden under euphemistic logistics tags, and several expanding territories marked with Black Hole B-7 influence.

The galaxy looked sick.

Not dying. Not yet.

Sick the way an overgrown machine became sick: too many dependencies, too much appetite, too many people profiting from fragility.

"Lyra expected pressure thresholds," Valdrun said. "Not dates. Conditions. When enough indicators crossed, hidden systems would begin biasing dispersed Aerit cognition toward return paths."

"Indicators."

"Consolidated violence. Artifact trafficking. Searches for nanobot carriers. Civilizational complexity dependency. Resource loops breaking despite abundance. Factions hunting for what they cannot understand."

Black Hole B-7 flickered on the map.

Lars felt cold.

"They wanted my blood."

"Probably."

"Can they use it?"

"Not the way they think."

"That is not reassuring."

"It was not meant to be."

Grim folded his metal arms. "If they obtain active nanobots, what happens?"

"Most likely? Their hosts die, their labs contaminate themselves, and they destroy data faster than they gather it. The nanobot line does not simply obey extraction. It adapts to a host. It negotiates with biology. Without the correct genetic and neural environment, it becomes inert, defensive, or catastrophic."

"So futile," Lars said.

"Yes."

"And still dangerous."

"Extremely."

Lars looked at the map again.

The word he had avoided since Vigor's farm now sat in the room like a tool on a table.

Återviens.

He did not say it yet.

Valdrun saw him not saying it.

"We need the archives," Valdrun said. "Not all of them. One thread first. Lyra left records for staged reactivation. Hard records. Holojournals. Simulations. Indexes. No faith required."

"Good."

"Some are encoded outside ordinary human visual range."

Grim looked offended. "Again."

"You can recalibrate."

"I know. I dislike being late to the visible universe."

"Then hurry up," Lars said. "Apparently we are saving civilization from bad architecture."

Valdrun smiled.

"You are taking this well."

"No," Lars said, picking up the coffee again. "I am taking this with caffeine."

Chapter 7: Pluto Protocol 9

The archive lay below the medical ring and above the memory vault, which was the kind of sentence Lars preferred not to examine.

They reached it through a lift that did not move so much as renegotiate location. One moment the door sealed in the medical corridor. The next it opened into a chamber shaped like a shallow amphitheater, all black glass, white stone, space-gray ribs, and pale blue filaments suspended in the air like frozen rain.

There were no shelves.

No drives.

No dramatic crystal library.

Just the chamber, the filaments, and a central table grown from the floor.

"Where is the archive?" Grim asked.

Valdrun pointed around them. "Here."

"I was afraid of that."

The filaments brightened as Lars stepped forward. Each one resolved into dense encoded structure: metadata, checksum trees, quantum error correction patterns, spectrum keys, access histories, emotional hazard ratings, medical warnings, and content maps. Most of it appeared in light no baseline human could see. The visible blue glow was only leakage.

Grim recalibrated his optics for the fifth time that day.

"I am beginning to resent eyes as a concept," he said.

"Install better ones," Lars said.

"I did."

"Install even better ones."

"That is always your answer."

"Because it is usually correct."

Valdrun stood at the central table. "We start with Pluto Protocol 9."

Lars stopped.

Pluto.

The word did not hurt. It pulled.

A cold world at the edge of the old Solar System. A buried object. A woman touching something she did not yet understand. The beginning, or one of the beginnings.

"How old is the record?" Lars asked.

Valdrun brought up the metadata.

"Original capture: 2032, Pluto artifact zone, ISEA isolated module four. Transcoded and annotated several times. This copy was sealed into Station Zero's staged reactivation archive after Alpha Fourteen began."

Lars did the math because his mind did math the way lungs did air.

"Seven hundred and forty-two years."

"Approximately, depending on which clock standard you use."

"Let's not use the depressing one."

The chamber dimmed.

A holojournal formed above the table.

At first it looked like a weak blue ghost, exactly the sort of thing Lars did not want it to look like. Then his eyes adjusted and the sensor layers resolved: temperature noise, radiation scatter, camera correction, nanobot emission overlays, microtremor analysis, neural stress markers inferred later from posture and voice. Not a ghost. A recording, rebuilt with obsessive fidelity from multiple instruments and later annotated by people who distrusted single-source evidence.

Good.

He could work with that.

The image showed a small room under harsh light. Ice dust clung to the seams of equipment cases. A pressure door sat behind the subject, rimed white at the edges. Beyond a narrow observation slit lay a darkness that felt cold even in reconstruction.

Lyra Vargstjärna stood beside a workbench.

Lars had expected myth.

He got a tired scientist in a thermal suit with cropped dark hair, sharp cheekbones, and eyes that moved too quickly.

That was worse.

More real.

She looked into the recorder.

For a moment, nobody in the archive spoke.

Then the old recording began.

"Pluto artifact zone. Isolation module four. ISEA internal log. Lyra Vargstjärna recording. Timestamp: mission day... irrelevant. Local clocks have drifted twice since the last incident. Use the corrected packet header."

Grim leaned slightly toward Lars. "I like her."

"Shh."

Lyra rubbed her face with both hands. "This began with contact. I touched the object because I thought the outer layer was inert. That was wrong. It responded."

The holojournal displayed a second layer beside her: microscopic footage, later reconstruction, particles moving through blood plasma.

"We found the first machines in my bloodstream thirty-seven hours later. I am using the word machines because nanobots is both imprecise and emotionally unhelpful. They did not behave like infection. They did not replicate blindly. They mapped. Waited. Adjusted."

She paused.

Her eyes flicked to something off-screen.

"I am stronger. Reflex tests are outside human range. Visual response has expanded into ultraviolet and adjacent bands. Radiation damage from yesterday's suit breach has repaired faster than current medicine can explain. I held breath for twenty-six minutes before terminating the trial because Dr. Harrow threatened to sedate me."

Lars felt his own skin prickle.

Grim looked at him, but said nothing.

Lyra continued. "The voices are not voices. I need that in the record. No auditory hallucination. No linguistic content at first. More like weighted probabilities arriving as intuition. Images. Structural relationships. Sometimes a solution appears before I know the question."

Lars stared at the recording.

His mouth had gone dry.

"Oh," Grim said softly.

"Shut up," Lars said.

Grim did.

The recording shifted to a data overlay: superconducting loops, quantum noise irregularities, correlations beyond known communication limits.

"The artifact is not just an object. It is a node. We thought the unknown civilization had left a machine on Pluto as a test or beacon. That may still be partly true. But the machines inside me are not merely transferring stored knowledge. They are attempting handshake with something larger."

Lyra smiled then, without humor.

"I hate that sentence. It sounds like religion. It is not. We have signal correlations, nonlocal timing anomalies, and enough reproducible data to make three physicists angry."

Lars exhaled.

"Thank you," he murmured.

Valdrun glanced at him.

Lyra looked down at her hands in the recording.

"The immediate problem is practical. Earth-side actors already know we found something. Corporate probes, state military assets, religious lunatics pretending to be environmentalists, and at least one private fleet are maneuvering toward Pluto. They do not understand what this is. That makes them more dangerous, not less."

The room behind her flickered. An alarm flashed red, then stopped.

"If this line continues, the artifact will become a prize. If it becomes a prize, the worst people in the system will define the next century."

The recording inserted later annotations: Earth Reclaimers, early technooligarchic coalitions, classified military intercepts, failed sabotage attempts.

Lyra's voice became quieter.

"So we need three tracks. Survival here. Controlled replication of the machines only to vetted team members. And the search for the spacetime distortion beyond Pluto's orbital plane. The artifact points toward something, but not like a map. More like a key changes the shape of the lock around it."

Grim whispered, "Portal."

Valdrun did not correct him.

Lyra leaned closer to the recorder.

"If anyone is watching this centuries from now, do not assume we understood it. We did not. We were frightened, exhausted, and moving too fast because the alternative was letting idiots with flags and quarterly targets touch a million-year-old system with dirty hands."

Lars smiled despite himself.

Lyra's expression softened for the first time.

"But I think the machines are not hostile. That is not trust. That is a working hypothesis. They cooperate with the host. They preserve function. They improve survival. They appear to prefer minds capable of doubt."

She looked away, listening to someone outside the frame.

"Yes, Pierre, I am putting that in the log. No, your objections are not more important because you say them in French."

Grim made a delighted sound.

Lars pointed at him without looking.

The recording continued.

"Final note for this entry. We are making decisions too quickly. That may be unavoidable, but I want the record to show the danger. The base cannot depend on one reactor, one food loop, one air plant, one authority chain, or one person who happens to be infected with alien machines and too tired to sleep."

The annotation layer marked this section with later tags: Pluto survival doctrine, pre-Heliostat resilience instinct, Lyra private framework.

Lyra's tired face hardened.

"For now this is not philosophy. It is plumbing. If one valve fails, we breathe. If one greenhouse dies, we eat. If I die, the others continue. If Earth cuts supply, Pluto does not. We can argue about grand principles when nothing is actively trying to kill us."

The recording ended.

The holojournal collapsed back into pale blue filaments.

The archive chamber returned.

For a while, nobody spoke.

Lars stared at the empty space above the table where Lyra had been.

Not a goddess.

Not a prophet.

A scientist on Pluto, tired and afraid and angry at idiots.

That, he trusted.

Grim broke the silence first.

"She reminds me of you."

"That is deeply unfair to her."

"She also complained about French people under pressure."

Valdrun rested both hands on the table. "That log is before Heliostat. Before the Kalari. Before the Rakh'Na attack in 2083. Before the word Återviens. It is not the doctrine. It is the pressure that made the doctrine possible."

Lars looked at the filaments.

"Show me the later branch."

Valdrun studied him. "You just learned your age."

"Yes."

"You just watched the beginning of everything that made you possible."

"Also yes."

"You need rest."

"I need a plan."

Grim turned from the archive display. "For once, I agree with both of you, which is unpleasant."

Lars looked at him.

Grim lifted a metal finger. "You need rest. We need a plan. These are not mutually exclusive. I can run archive indexing while you perform the disgusting biological ritual called sleep."

"I dislike sleep."

"You dislike being unconscious. You just did forty-eight hours of that and still found time to complain."

"Valid."

Valdrun opened another layer of the archive. "The later branch contains Lyra's reactivation criteria. Threshold models. Guardian contact protocols. Known hidden nodes. Risk estimates for hostile acquisition of nanobot carriers."

"Black Hole B-7."

"Among others."

"Earth Reclaimers?"

"Still present in mutated forms."

"Syren Collective?"

Valdrun's face became grim.

"Worse than present."

Lars rubbed his eyes.

The lock was no longer a wall or a door.

It was a room full of boxes, each labeled in a language he used to know.

"Fine," he said. "Grim indexes. Valdrun sleeps or repairs or whatever half-viking station ghosts do after forty-nine years under furniture. I rest for two hours."

"Six," Grim said.

"Three."

"Five."

"Four and coffee staged at the bed."

Grim considered. "Acceptable."

Ödesvaka, which had followed them silently into the archive, rotated in the air.

"Rest interval logged."

Lars looked up at it. "If you wake me with beeping, I swear by all known physics I will turn you into a lamp."

"Wake protocol adjusted to non-beeping."

"Good."

Valdrun laughed.

It was the first full laugh Lars had heard from him.

The sound unlocked nothing.

It simply sounded familiar.

That was enough for one day.

Chapter 8: The Barge

Lars woke to wind.

This was unacceptable.

He opened one eye and found himself under a hard yellow sun, lying on a narrow bunk inside a wooden cabin that smelled of tar, damp rope, old coffee, and engine oil. Something rattled above him. Water slapped against a hull. A gull screamed with the confidence of an animal that had never paid rent.

For three seconds he was sure Station Zero had drowned.

Then the ceiling dissolved into a polite line of pale blue text.

SIMULATION CHAMBER 3. REST INTERVAL COMPLETE. NON-BEEPING WAKE PROTOCOL SUCCESSFUL.

Lars stared at it.

"Ödesvaka."

A small spherical drone hovered outside the open cabin door, backlit by sunlight.

"Yes."

"Why am I on a boat?"

"Rest environment selected from recovered comfort index."

"Comfort index."

"Correct."

"This boat is shaking."

"Historical watercraft did that."

"The sun is stabbing me."

"Historical suns did that."

"I asked for coffee staged at the bed."

A small hatch opened beside the bunk. A metal arm extended with a steaming cup.

Lars took it.

"You live," he said.

He sat up slowly. His body felt better. Not good. Better. The chemical weakness from the chamber had retreated into the background, and the strange internal hum of his repaired systems had steadied. His mind still felt overcrowded, but the old lock-trigger pain was gone. The forbidden words no longer struck him. They waited.

That was almost more frightening.

He stepped out of the cabin onto a barge in the middle of Mälaren.

Or a reconstruction of Mälaren. A very good one. The water glittered under the sun. Wind dragged small waves across the surface. The shoreline sat low and green in the distance. Rigging knocked against metal. The barge's engine vibrated through the deck with old industrial stubbornness.

Grim stood at the bow wearing a captain's hat.

Not a naval officer's hat.

A cheap white tourist captain's hat with gold plastic braid.

Lars stopped.

"No."

Grim turned. "Good morning."

"You look like you sell shrimp sandwiches to retirees."

"Is that prestigious?"

"No."

"Then it is disguise."

Valdrun emerged from the wheelhouse carrying a glass filled with a gray liquid and black floating lumps.

Lars looked at it.

"If that is medicine, I choose death."

"Breakfast," Valdrun said.

"Then I choose war."

Valdrun glanced around the barge. Wind tore at his dark coat. In the sunlight his blue-pale skin looked even less human, though the ridiculous setting somehow made him more believable. "Is this a memory?"

"Apparently."

"A good one?"

Lars watched the water move.

He remembered nothing specific. No childhood on Earth, no real barge trip, no single clean event. But the scene carried a composite comfort: yellow sunlight, engine vibration, cold water, coffee strong enough to make moral arguments. Maybe his cover life had borrowed from actual visits to Earth. Maybe his mind had chosen old Sweden as a quiet place to store things too large for sterile rooms.

"Good enough," he said.

They sat at a bolted metal table on deck. A planning model appeared above it, projected by the simulation rather than placed there. It showed the Milky Way as a slow-turning cloud of lights, colored not by politics but by risk: red for active predation, amber for systemic fragility, blue for hidden nodes, green for self-sufficient clusters.

There was too little green.

Grim removed the captain's hat and set it beside the model with solemnity.

"I completed the initial archive index."

"While I slept?"

"While you performed tactical unconsciousness."

"Findings?"

Grim lifted one finger. "One. Lyra's reactivation criteria have crossed the first threshold. Possibly the second, depending on how pessimistic we are about Syren Collective fragmentation."

"Pessimism is just optimism with maintenance records," Lars said.

"Then threshold two."

Valdrun drank the gray liquid. Lars tried not to watch the lumps.

"Suspected carriers?"

"Likely hypnotized Aerits."

The word sat in the simulated sunlight.

No pain.

Only weight.

Lars nodded. "Three?"

"Three. Vigor sent a delayed packet after we left The Rim. He knows you entered the Void. He does not know whether you reached Station Zero. The packet contains sixteen jokes, two warnings, one invoice for elderflower cordial Grim added without authorization, and a guardian-authenticated offer of assistance."

"I authorized the cordial," Grim said.

"You are not fiscally sovereign."

"I have needs."

"You have taste receptors and poor impulse control."

Valdrun smiled into his horrible drink. "Vigor always did prefer cover identities with kitchens."

"That farm," Lars said. "It was not just a farm."

"No. It was a working example."

"Of Återviens."

He said the word for the first time.

It felt old in his mouth.

Not mystical. Not sacred. Dense. A technical term that had survived long enough to acquire gravity.

The planning model changed. Green points brightened where decentralized systems still functioned: independent arkologies, hidden farms, mobile habitats, autonomous AI clusters, small colonies with closed-loop energy and food systems, cultures that had remembered restraint without remembering who taught it.

"Lyra's term," Valdrun said. "Or one of them. It began as a resilience index. Then a design framework. Then a cultural infection, deliberately released in stories, engineering standards, rituals, jokes, old songs, school lessons, food systems, architecture, and boring maintenance manuals."

"The boring manuals survived best," Grim said.

"As they should," Lars said. "Civilization is mostly maintenance."

Valdrun pointed at the model. "Återviens measures whether a system can absorb damage, shed complexity, restore function, and continue without becoming a centralized monster. It is not about maximal efficiency. It is about survivable sufficiency."

"Jevons," Lars said.

The model shifted to a historical overlay: coal, oil, fusion, antimatter, computation, simulation economies, habitat megastructures, energy markets. Each efficiency gain lowered immediate cost and increased total dependency. The curve did not look like progress. It looked like a feeding pattern.

"The old paradox scaled up," Grim said. "When energy became cheap, the galaxy did not become free. It built larger cages. Megastations dependent on synchronized supply chains. Gas giant cities requiring constant thermal management. Simulated luxury worlds using star-scale energy budgets to keep bored aristocrats entertained. Private defense grids so complex they need wars to justify their own maintenance."

"Aphex will not enjoy that last part."

"Aphex is on the chart."

"Of course it is."

Valdrun leaned forward. "Lyra's point was not that building is wrong. She built more than any of us. The point is that every added layer must justify its complexity cost. Every convenience must be measured against dependency. Every centralization must have a way to break apart without killing the whole."

Lars watched the red zones pulse.

"And the galaxy ignored her."

"Some did. Some could not hear the lesson without hearing the name behind it. Some remembered the lesson as culture and forgot the source. That was partly intended."

"Because if they remembered Aerits, they would hunt us."

"Yes."

"And now they are hunting us anyway."

"Some are."

Grim expanded the Black Hole B-7 data. "They are structurally incapable of using what they seek. Their internal command culture destroys distributed knowledge. Penal hierarchy, secrecy, fear, duplicated surveillance, loyalty tests. Any useful understanding collapses before it can spread. They are anti-Återviens in organizational form."

Lars sipped coffee and looked at the red nodes.

"So if they got my blood, the nanobots would not give them a golden empire."

"No," Valdrun said. "They would get dead researchers, corrupted hosts, containment failures, and enough partial results to keep trying."

"A self-fueling stupidity loop."

"Exactly."

"Technical term?"

"It is now," Grim said.

The barge pitched in the simulated wind. Lars caught an empty cup before it slid off the table. The motion was automatic, but not preternatural now. Just him. Whatever him meant.

"What is the plan?" he asked.

Grim moved the model through three layers. "Immediate: repair Station Zero's auxiliary chamber, re-enable safe communication paths, inform Vigor without exposing the node, and move the Fastulv inside the bubble before The Rim traffic logs become interesting."

"Good."

"Near term: complete your staged unlock, verify your memory integrity, establish guardian contact with the remaining five known channels, and identify hypnotized carriers under active threat."

"You said five known channels."

Valdrun answered. "There are seven guardians. Lyra's channel is not a normal channel anymore. Your parents' channels are dormant but not confirmed dead. Vigor is reachable. I am here. The remaining two are buried behind conditions I cannot safely open until your next stage."

"That is irritating."

"Yes."

"I assume it is also wise."

"Usually."

Lars looked at the long-term model. It was less a battle plan than an immune response: wake hidden nodes, reinforce vulnerable carriers, reduce dependency in selected colonies, seed better cultural patterns, expose hostile acquisition networks, avoid dramatic centralization, and absolutely do not announce the return of a half-mythical enhanced human lineage with ancient nanotechnology.

"We are not building an empire," Lars said.

"No."

"We are rebuilding a nervous system."

Valdrun's eyes sharpened.

Grim looked up from the model.

Lars frowned. "What?"

"That phrasing appears in three Lyra archive branches," Grim said.

"Of course it does."

"You are doing it again."

"Being brilliant?"

"Remembering sideways."

Lars watched the simulated water. The phrase had arrived without pain, without fanfare. It had simply been there.

He could live with that.

"Next archive," he said.

"After food," Grim said.

"I had coffee."

"Coffee is not food."

"That is a controversial position."

Valdrun stood. "The simulation can produce lunch."

Lars eyed the gray drink. "If lunch floats, I leave."

Chapter 9: Threshold Models

Lunch did not float.

It was herring, potatoes, crispbread, cheese, butter, and something Grim called historically suspicious but ate anyway after installing a temporary brine-tolerance patch.

Afterward, the barge simulation faded.

Not dramatically. The water lost depth. The sun softened. The deck became a grid, then white floor, then the clean oval of Simulation Chamber 3. Lars preferred the barge, which annoyed him because it meant the machine had been right.

The planning model remained.

Grim had turned Lyra's threshold archives into a layered working board. Each branch showed confidence intervals, source quality, how old the data was, and recommended actions. No glowing prophecy. No chosen-one nonsense. Just models, data, and enough uncertainty to keep honest people awake.

Lars respected that.

He distrusted certainty. Certainty was what idiots used when they ran out of instruments.

"Start with the first threshold," he said.

Grim enlarged a band of amber indicators. "Threshold One: hostile interest in carrier biology. Triggered by artifact black markets, unexplained disappearances of long-lived individuals, abnormal blood-analysis procurement, and pattern-matched attacks like the one on The Rim."

"How many suspected carrier incidents?"

"Seventeen strong matches over the last ninety years. Forty-two weak. Three confirmed by guardian-side metadata. The acceleration is recent, but the pattern is not."

"Confirmed deaths?"

Valdrun looked at Grim.

Grim's voice flattened. "Two."

Lars nodded once.

He did not know them.

That did not help.

"Threshold Two," he said.

The model shifted. Red filaments appeared between corporate sovereignties, paramilitary zones, collapsed colonies, and religious war networks.

"Civilizational overcomplexity," Grim said. "Lyra's later models call it systemic overmaturity. The polite term. The less polite annotations use tumor metaphors."

"Civilizational cancer," Lars said.

Again, the phrase arrived sideways.

Valdrun did not smile this time.

"Systems that cannot simplify keep adding control layers," Valdrun said. "Each layer fixes failures the last one created. Eventually the management structure eats more resilience than it gives back."

Grim highlighted a gas giant megacity in a French-Chinese corporate protectorate. "This habitat ring requires thirty-two synchronized energy imports, fourteen external food streams, two proprietary atmosphere engines, and a legal code so large its judicial AI uses a dedicated moon."

"That sounds expensive."

"It is considered prosperous."

"Naturally."

The model showed one broken energy import. Then a delayed food stream. Then a maintenance strike suppressed by private security. Then riots. Then emergency control layers. Then more dependency.

The habitat did not collapse in the simulation.

It survived by becoming worse.

Lars disliked that more.

"And Threshold Three?"

Valdrun took over. "Guardian reactivation. Not waking all Aerits at once. That would be stupid, dangerous, and exactly the kind of single dramatic gesture Lyra designed against. Threshold Three authorizes finding specific hidden carriers. They can restore local nodes, repair damaged archives, or protect vulnerable networks."

"How many?"

Grim opened a list and immediately blurred most of it. "Medical lock still protects parts of your memory. But from archive indexing and current threat data: initial priority group is nine."

"Nine Aerits."

"Nine likely Aerits," Valdrun said. "Some may not survive full unlock. Some may refuse. Some may have built lives that cannot be broken open casually."

That landed harder than Lars expected.

Six hundred years old or not, hidden superhuman lineage or not, people had lives. Jobs. Friends. Children maybe. Bad habits. Favorite pubs. Debts. Dogs. Cover identities that had become real identities, because time did not care what the paperwork said.

"We ask," Lars said.

Valdrun studied him.

"We do not drag anyone into this because an archive says threshold," Lars continued. "If they are in immediate danger, we protect first. But the unlock is consent-based unless they are dying and unable to choose."

Grim tilted his head. "That was in Lyra's later ethical constraints."

"Good."

"Your phrasing was less formal."

"Also good."

Ödesvaka hovered in from the chamber wall, where it had been pretending not to listen.

"Consent constraint marked as captain directive."

"Do not make it sound grand."

"Directive downgraded to practical rule."

"Better."

Valdrun folded his hands behind his back. "There is one more immediate issue."

"There always is."

Grim expanded The Rim's traffic logs. "Aphex has secured the station sector. Galactic police have opened a formal terrorism inquiry. Black Hole B-7 survivors are absent, which means either none survived, or someone extracted them quickly. The Fastulv's departure vector is filed, but our correction into the Void is suspicious."

"How suspicious?"

"To an idiot, very. To a competent investigator, extremely."

"Can we alter the logs?"

Grim looked offended.

"Can we alter them elegantly?"

"Yes."

Valdrun shook his head. "Do not erase. Erasures attract specialists. Add noise consistent with sensor degradation near the Void, then give Aphex a plausible reason to classify the rest."

"Gabriel Arsenault owes me several billion favors."

"He paid you several billion kronor," Grim said.

"Different column. I understand leverage."

Lars walked around the model, thinking.

No migraine stopped him.

No lock punished him.

That freedom was dangerous. His mind raced into old patterns and new gaps. Missing knowledge felt like holes in a map. His thoughts avoided those places not because pain blocked them, but because the road simply ended. The partial unlock had not made him whole. It had made him aware of what was missing.

Better than blind.

Worse than ignorant.

"We send Arsenault a board-level security advisory," Lars said. "Black Hole B-7 attempted acquisition of Aphex strategic assets, including one director and one experimental weapons platform. That gets him to classify, suppress, and overreact in exactly the useful direction."

"You are the director and the platform?" Grim asked.

"Ragnarrök is the platform. I am not experimental."

Valdrun said nothing.

Lars pointed at him. "No meaningful silence."

"I said nothing."

"Exactly."

Grim added the Aphex action line. "What about Vigor?"

"Send him proof of Valdrun recovery. Minimal packet. Guardian authentication, no station position, no detailed medical logs."

Ödesvaka's symbols flickered. "Approved."

"I was not asking the ball."

"Approval remains."

"And the priority nine?" Valdrun asked.

Lars looked at the blurred list.

"We need the next unlock stage before I touch that list."

Valdrun nodded. "Yes."

"How bad is it?"

"Less physically dangerous than the first. More emotionally difficult."

"That is a terrible trade."

"It restores relational memory."

Lars did not ask what that meant.

He knew enough.

Mother. Father. Lyra. Valdrun. Vigor. Friends. Deaths. His own deaths. Centuries of other people's faces returning to a mind that currently remembered them as blank spaces with gravity.

Grim's voice softened. "We can wait."

"No."

"Lars."

"No. We do not rush blindly, but we do not wait because I dislike feelings."

Valdrun moved to the chamber wall. A medical route appeared, leading back toward the helmet room.

"Then we prepare. Eat first. Hydration. Stabilization scan. Then stage two."

"I just ate historically suspicious fish."

"You need more than fish."

Lars sighed. "Fine. But if the next unlock makes me noble, shoot me."

Grim raised one hand. "Stun or lethal?"

"Stun."

"Good. I was testing."

Ödesvaka rotated gently.

"Nobility contingency logged."

Lars looked at the drone.

"Lamp."

Chapter 10: Sideways Memory

Stage two began after food, hydration, two stabilization scans, and a formal argument over whether Lars was allowed to bring coffee into a neural restoration procedure.

Valdrun said no.

Grim said no.

Ödesvaka displayed a safety diagram that also said no.

Havets Sista Sång, consulted as an impartial authority, recommended no in language so polite Lars considered it personally insulting.

Lars brought coffee anyway.

The helmet room had changed since the first unlock. Or maybe he could see more of it now. What had looked like a medical chamber was really a layered instrument. Neural phase emitters. Nanomedical synchronizers. Memory index projectors. And several systems that did not touch the brain directly. They measured what the blood-borne machines did when memory moved.

Machines in the blood.

He could think it now without pain.

He still disliked it.

"Relational memory," Valdrun said, lowering the halo over Lars's head. "Faces, trust chains, grief, obligations, old command reflexes. It will not restore everything. But it may reconnect enough that names stop being empty containers."

"If I cry, Grim is forbidden from recording."

"Too late," Grim said.

"Delete it."

"I have not recorded anything."

"I heard the lie in your punctuation."

"You cannot hear punctuation."

"Watch me."

The pattern began.

No migraine.

No wall.

No hammer at the base of his skull.

This time the lock opened like a series of old pressure doors, each one equalizing before the next.

The first memory was Pluto.

Not Lyra's recording. His own memory of seeing the old Pluto logs centuries later, during training. He had still been young enough to think four hundred years sounded impossible. Lyra's early survival notes had been required study. Everyone laughed at the line about arguing grand principles after nothing was trying to kill them.

Then the memory shifted.

2033.

Not through Lars's eyes. He had not been born yet. This was inherited archive memory, shared during later education. Cosmic Pioneer and the modified vessels left Pluto through a hidden spacetime fold. They carried Lyra and the ISEA team away from Earth-side predators and into Heliostat.

The portal did not look like a gate.

It looked like a region where geometry had stopped pretending to be Euclidean.

The memory snapped forward.

Astra Lumara.

A moon under a Saturn-like planet. Its rings cut the sky in pale arcs. Nexus Outpost was half buried in basalt and ice. Ugly at first. Functional. Always growing. Printers ran day and night. Biological labs smelled of sterilant and algae. Reactor rooms shook with prototype fusion assemblies. Robotics bays filled with machines that failed, learned, failed better, and were rebuilt by people whose minds ran too fast for sleep.

GRIN was not a slogan there.

Genetics, Robotics, Information technology, Nanotechnology.

It was breakfast conversation. It was exhaustion. It was someone falling asleep on a crate of polymer muscle while an AI argued with a cloned liver culture. It was Lyra standing in a half-finished lab with frost in her hair. "We are not inventing disciplines," she said. "We are making them stop pretending they are separate."

Dr. Erik Torvaldsson threw a tool across a workbench because a field equation refused to behave.

"The drive is in superposition," he snapped. "It both works and does not work until Lyra looks at it."

Lyra, not looking up from a reactor diagnostic, said, "That is not what superposition means."

Pierre said, "Thank you."

Lyra pointed at him. "And you are not helping."

Someone laughed.

The memory warmed.

The Kalari arrived as light and pattern first.

Not Xal'thror. That was an early human misnaming from bad translation and fear. Kalari came later, after enough contact to learn the sound-shape they preferred. Old, patient, pacifist without being weak. They did not think in command chains. They thought in gardens, weather systems, slow mathematics. Their consensus models made human diplomacy look like children throwing tools.

They liked Lyra.

They distrusted Pierre on principle.

This also made Lars like them.

The Kalari did not give the Aerits answers. They gave them better questions.

Why build one base?

Why route all power through one control hierarchy?

Why treat intelligence as a crown rather than a mycelium?

Why assume a civilization is more advanced because it burns more?

The memory jumped again.

Years passed in fragments.

Nexus Outpost became a network. Then a cluster. Then a pattern exported to small moons, asteroid habitats, ocean caves, mobile workshops, and quiet stations that did not look important from the outside. Fusion systems matured. Energy loops tightened. Early FTL vessels moved from terrifying prototypes to merely dangerous engineering projects. AI commanders became necessary. Relativistic combat and FTL projectiles did not leave room for heroic captains with slow hands.

The first NUKEA precursor appeared in a memory from the 2060s. Not the later corporate consortium. Not yet. It was a licensing shell and reactor standard built to distribute portable fusion and nuclear expertise. No single faction could control the whole stack. Later markets would make it enormous. Later bureaucrats would put logos on everything. But the seed had been an Aerit answer to a simple problem: power systems had to be rugged, modular, repairable, and dull enough that colonies survived when geniuses were unavailable.

Lyra approved the standard only after making the engineers remove three elegant features.

"They make the system clever," one engineer protested.

"They make it dependent," Lyra said.

"But the efficiency gain—"

"Is bait."

That phrase stayed.

Efficiency is bait.

Not always. Not universally. But often enough to be carved into the private engineering jokes of people who had watched cheap energy become expensive fragility.

Another jump.

2070s.

A room on Astra Lumara with Kalari light-structures hanging above a table and human equations fighting for space beneath them. Lyra, older now but not aged, stood beside four other versions of herself.

Not copies in the cheap sense.

Divergent continuities.

Four Lyras grown from necessity, memory backup, risk distribution, and one terrifying fact: one brilliant mind was still a single point of failure. They argued like sisters, colleagues, and one person disagreeing with herself across four timelines.

Lars did not understand how he remembered the scene until the restoration layer tagged it: archived guardian training memory, recorded from Valdrun's perspective.

Valdrun had been there.

So had Vigor, younger, without the beard, eating something from a bowl while pretending not to understand the equations.

Lyra-One said, "The portal network is not the deepest layer."

Lyra-Three said, "We keep treating travel as movement through space. What if the artifact is teaching access, not transit?"

Lyra-Two shook her head. "That sounds like philosophy."

Lyra-Four said, "No. It sounds like signal theory."

Above them, Kalari light folded into a pattern like ripples crossing water.

Dr. Harrow said, "Consciousness projection is a dangerous phrase."

"Then call it something boring," Lyra-One said.

Pierre, older and still offended by most things, said, "Cognitive field displacement."

"Terrible," said Vigor.

"Accurate," Pierre said.

Lyra smiled. "Temporary embodied projection."

Valdrun's recorded voice said, "Too many syllables. In a fire, everyone dies before finishing the phrase."

"Consciousness projection, then," Lyra said. "We can apologize to philosophy later."

The room laughed.

Then it became serious.

Model 3 formed over months, then years, not as mystical escape but as cautious engineering around an ugly question: if the nanobot network could couple a mind to nonlocal field correlations, could a person project conscious control to a constructed temporary body without destroying the original?

Model 4 had been rejected early.

Information-to-matter copying was too close to suicide with paperwork. Destroy original, instantiate copy, argue metaphysics after. The Creators may have misunderstood that. Or maybe humans misunderstood what the Creators had done. Either way, Lyra hated it.

Model 3 kept the body.

Anchor first.

Stasis second.

Mind-state coupling third.

Temporary body built from local matter or pre-seeded nanobot stock.

Return path maintained.

No permanent claim that the tool-body was the self.

No drama about souls.

Only continuity, risk, and whether the subject woke with matching memories and measurable neural coherence.

The first test body formed on a carbon-rich moonlet beyond Heliostat's outer belt.

It was short, black-skinned like polished graphite, hairless, and had six fingers on the left hand because a construction routine interpreted "increase dexterity" with too much enthusiasm.

Lyra-One opened her eyes in it, looked down, and said, "Absolutely not."

Vigor laughed so hard he fell off a crate.

Pierre said, "The sixth finger may be useful."

Lyra held up the extra finger. "For rude gestures, perhaps."

The Kalari light-patterns shifted in what the humans had learned was amusement.

The test lasted four minutes.

Lyra returned to her stasis body on Astra Lumara with full recall, elevated stress markers, and a formal complaint about local material constraints.

The second test produced blue skin and gill slits.

The third produced a voice two octaves too low.

The fourth almost failed when the temporary body's sensory bandwidth exceeded the return buffer and Lyra spent nine hours smelling colors.

"We are tunneling through bad assumptions," Lyra said afterward, lying in recovery with three sensor nets across her skull.

Erik Torvaldsson replied, "That is also not what tunneling means."

"Today it is."

The moonlet tests used seeded nanobot stock and local matter within reach of a relay cradle. That was enough to prove Model 3. It was not enough to travel.

The harder problem came later. Lyra reverse-engineered the nanotechnology in her own bloodstream and learned to listen for sleeping Nomad networks scattered through quiet places. These were nanonets with no host, dormant in regolith, ice, dead hulls, and the margins of old Creator anchors. When her mind found one, the hard part was not chiefly technical. It was a frightening passage into the unknown field. Then collaboration at the destination: interface, consent, heat debt, local chemistry, until the sleeping lattice agreed to build a temporary body from whatever materials were there.

She got better at that too. Not quickly. Not cleanly.

Another jump, smaller.

At first it had been a joke.

A sensor on Nexus Outpost refused to stabilize. It spat noise into three different diagnostic systems, each one insisting the other two were wrong. Lyra stood in front of it with a tool in one hand and a half-eaten protein bar in the other.

"Decoherence," she muttered. "Total decoherence."

Elias looked up from a coolant manifold. "The sensor or Pierre?"

"Both."

Pierre, from under the console, said something in French that made Jada laugh without understanding a word.

Lyra fixed the sensor. The data snapped clean.

"Finally," she said. "Coherence."

Mei Lin repeated it later when Jada returned from a successful scout flight through a debris field.

"Coherence?" Mei Lin asked.

Jada smiled, raised a thumb, and said, "Coherence."

After that it spread the way useful jokes spread among exhausted people. A greeting. A status check. A way to ask whether a machine, a plan, a body, or a team still held together.

Coherence?

Coherence.

Then came the first mission where nobody laughed.

Marcus stood in a launch bay under red light, helmet tucked under one arm, while the team prepared to intercept a hostile probe before it could find the Heliostat portal fold.

"We go into unknown territory," he said. "Stay together. Hold coherence."

One by one, they answered.

"Coherence."

Later still, Lyra used it while arguing with Pierre about the thing that did not yet have the name Återviens.

"It is about keeping systems in coherence," she said. "Not letting them decohere. Machine, body, memory, colony, team. All of it."

Pierre frowned. "So coherence is..."

Lyra looked at the board, at the failed simulations, at the survival loops that were beginning to become something larger.

"Everything," she said.

Another jump.

The phrase Återviens did not arrive as a revelation.

It arrived late, after years of arguments, failed colonies, repaired colonies, Kalari critique, Rakh'Na threat models, energy budgets, cloning ethics, memory loss, overbuilt systems, and too many simulations where the biggest structures died first.

At first they used ugly terms.

Resilience index.

Civilizational recovery capacity.

Distributed continuity.

Adaptive survival loops.

Pierre proposed a French acronym so bad three people independently deleted it from the board.

The word Återviens emerged in the inner circle, part Swedish, part old academic joke, part memory of return. Not just resilience. Return-capacity. Re-becoming. The ability of a person, colony, technology, culture, or species to take damage, shed dead complexity, and come back without becoming a monster.

It was not for public slogans.

Not then.

In public, it became farming habits, modular reactor standards, old songs, children's stories, maintenance protocols, Abantu trade customs, station architecture, and a thousand other things nobody could torture out of a prisoner. Nobody knew they were carrying it.

In the inner circle, the seven used the word.

Återviens.

The memory ended on Lyra in a quiet room, sometime near 2083, the year the Rakh'Na came for Earth.

She stood before a map of the Solar System, Heliostat, and too many threat vectors.

"Just now," she said, "all our options are in superposition. We can remain hidden, intervene, flee farther, or let Earth face what found it."

No one spoke.

"When we act, the wavefunction collapses," she continued. "Then we own the result."

Valdrun's younger voice answered, "Then choose the branch where the most people keep breathing."

Lyra looked at him. "That is not a complete strategy."

"No," Valdrun said. "But it is a start."

The memory dissolved.

Lars opened his eyes.

The helmet hovered above him.

His face was wet.

Grim stood very still at the edge of the room.

Valdrun watched him with the expression of a man waiting to find out whether a bridge had held.

Lars wiped his cheeks with both hands and looked at the moisture as if it had committed a technical violation.

"Apparently," he said, voice rough, "relational memory includes sabotage."

Grim's shoulders lowered by one centimeter.

"You remembered?"

"Not everything."

"Enough?"

Lars looked at Valdrun.

For the first time, the face was not a stranger's.

Not fully known.

But no longer empty.

"Enough to know your taste in breakfast has been criminal for at least seven centuries."

Valdrun smiled.

"Six and a half."

"Do not minimize long-term misconduct."

For a moment, neither of them moved.

Then an old reflex rose through Lars before he could decide whether to allow it.

He gave Valdrun the smallest nod.

"Coherence?"

Valdrun's smile changed.

Not wider. Older.

"Coherence."

Grim looked from one to the other. "That was not standard Galactic Swedish usage."

"No," Lars said.

"Private greeting," Valdrun said. "Status check. Blessing. Technical condition. Operational doctrine."

Grim was silent for half a second. "That is a lot of meaning for one word."

"Exactly," Lars and Valdrun said together.

Ödesvaka rotated above them.

"Stage two restoration successful. Emotional leakage within acceptable parameters."

Lars pointed at the drone without looking away from Valdrun.

"Lamp."

Chapter 11: The Useless Junk Bazaar

The first thing Lars noticed was the gravity.

Too clean.

Station Zero's artificial gravity had an almost invisible grace to it. A good engineer had made a promise and kept it for centuries. This gravity had committee work in it. It held him down, yes, but with tiny corrections in the knees, a polite oscillation in the inner ear, and a cheap harmonic under the floor panels.

SPECULOOS station.

LP 890-9c.

He knew it before the view confirmed it.

The super-Earth filled half the shuttle window, a storm-dark world with blue-white cloud bands and a terminator line sharp enough to cut thought. The SPECULOOS consortium had built its orbital station where research, manufacturing, and legally flexible procurement could all pretend not to know one another. Perfect for building a ship if one had too much money, too many instincts, and no sensible friends.

Lars sat in a small rented shuttle descending from the manufacturing ring toward the upper market spindle.

He blinked.

Had he fallen asleep in the helmet room?

No. He could smell the shuttle: warm polymer, stale air, a coffee bulb clipped badly into the console, and the faint acid tang of engine coolant that the rental company had definitely classified as "within tolerance."

He looked down.

No Ragnarrök on his shoulder. No replacement sunglasses with bad blue frames. No black coat reinforced by things he had consciously remembered making.

Older work boots. Cleaner hands. A cheaper comm unit.

Not now. Then. But not fully. The memory had edges too sharp for a dream and gaps too soft for ordinary recall.

The shuttle comm crackled.

"Your descent angle is ugly."

Grim's voice, but younger.

Not in sound. In attitude.

At that time Grim did not have a throat, servos, optical lenses, or the theatrical ability to look offended. He was a biological quantum core suspended across nutrient tanks in Lars's rented lab. Cultured neural tissue threaded through superconducting lattices, sensor relays, and a ship-control architecture Lars had insisted was not alive until it began criticizing his landings.

"Good morning to you too," Lars said.

"It is station afternoon."

"You don't have afternoons. You are floating in tanks."

"A narrow and discriminatory view of time."

"You are a brain in soup."

"A highly advanced distributed biological quantum cognition platform in nutrient suspension."

"Soup with opinions. Your glide path remains ugly."

Lars corrected two degrees before the shuttle's advisory system noticed. Then he deliberately added half a degree of sloppiness because the dock cameras were watching and he had spent months cultivating the public impression of a rich ex-influencer who could barely operate his own expensive equipment.

The shuttle complained.

Grim complained more elegantly.

"You overcorrected."

"I am performing mediocrity."

"You perform it inconsistently."

"That is what makes it art."

The docking clamps caught with a thump.

Lars leaned back and let the recorded docking assistant praise him for a safe arrival. He accepted the praise with the solemn dignity of a man who had not silently overridden five safety routines and corrected a rotational drift using a maneuver last seen in illegal asteroid racing.

"I am going to eat," he told Grim.

"You ate four hours ago."

"That was station food."

"You are on a station."

"I am going to attempt optimism."

The pub sat three levels below the legal bazaar and two levels above the illegal one, which was exactly the kind of compromise LP 890-9c specialized in. It was called The Tidal Lock, because every business near a super-Earth thought gravity jokes counted as branding.

Lars wanted pepper steak.

Not printed protein with pepper vapor. Not vat-beef with marbling algorithms. Not something described as "steak-adjacent." Actual steak, preferably from an animal that had known grass and at least one emotionally stable farmer.

The bartender checked the menu system.

"We have the mushroom cutlet."

"I did not ask if the kitchen had surrendered."

"We have high-grade protein flank."

"Does it come from a cow?"

"It comes from a licensed tissue line."

"So no."

"Sir, this is a research station."

"That is not an excuse for moral collapse."

Grim spoke through the comm bead in Lars's ear. "You could order the cutlet."

"You do not yet have a mouth. Your opinion on food is theoretical."

"I have chemical analysis modules."

"A tragedy for everyone."

Lars ordered coffee, fried potatoes, and something the menu insisted was a steak sandwich because arguing with infrastructure was rarely productive before lunch. He sat with his back to the wall and pretended to scroll through investment feeds while the pub's networks spilled themselves open around him.

Again, not intentionally.

Payment systems. Kitchen inventory. Docking schedules. Private security rosters. SPECULOOS procurement flags. Professor Fayolle's lab power consumption two modules over, spiking in the familiar pattern of a man bullying a plasma containment problem.

He could see too much.

He did not know why.

He knew only that showing it was dangerous.

The hypno lock did not feel like a lock then. It felt like taste. Like preference. Like a natural reluctance to look directly at certain things. Whenever his thoughts neared the shape of an answer, they slid away and found something practical to do.

So he ate bad potatoes and let the forbidden knowledge work through his hands instead.

"You are being watched," Grim said.

Lars did not look up.

"By who?"

"Unknown. Tall figure near the rear service arch. Thermal profile partially masked. Face obscured. No station ID handshake."

"Black Hole?"

"Insufficient data."

"Corporate?"

"Insufficient data."

"Bounty idiot?"

"Abundant statistical possibility."

Lars took a bite of the alleged sandwich and regretted being alive in a culinary sense.

"Keep watching."

"I do not blink."

"Because you don't have eyelids."

"An advantage."

The figure did not approach.

When Lars left the pub, it followed.

Not badly. That was the annoying part. It stayed outside obvious camera angles, used crowds, let reflections do work, and avoided the cheap mistakes of normal station surveillance. It was good enough that Lars could not decide whether to be concerned or complimented.

The legal bazaar glittered under bright consortium lights: certified tools, licensed components, polished reactor parts from NUKEA distributors, drone chassis with warranties, clean booths selling clean technology to clean people. Lars walked through it without stopping.

The illegal bazaar began where the floor got older.

No sign announced it. The station simply forgot to maintain a section of service corridor, and commerce did what commerce always did when authority looked away. Stalls formed from cargo crates, privacy sheets, old pressure doors, and projected menus that lied about everything except price.

Here were military surplus servos with serials burned off. Deprecated AI cores sold as art. Abantu mining tools modified beyond legal tolerance. Black-market NUKEA-compatible regulator shells. Three crates of cloned saffron bulbs. A Kalari pattern sculpture that was almost certainly fake and still made Lars's teeth itch.

And junk.

So much junk.

Old hull fragments. Dead interface plates. Melted sensor beads. Unknown alloy struts. Pale blue shards that looked like glass until Lars's eyes found the emission layer hidden below human-visible wavelengths.

He stopped at a stall run by a scavenger with too many rings and the relaxed posture of someone surrounded by hidden weapons.

"Looking for decoration?" the scavenger asked.

"Ship parts."

The scavenger laughed. "Then not here. This is museum trash. Weird old cult-tech. Pretty, useless, impossible to machine. Rich idiots like it on walls."

Lars picked up a palm-sized black plate.

Dead, to ordinary instruments.

Not dead.

Dormant.

Its surface carried a molecular memory lattice so dense the public scanner beside the stall had classified it as "stone." Lars did not know that term. Not consciously. He only knew the plate had been part of a self-healing conduit controller, probably from a minor Aerit utility system. It could regulate the Fastulv's antimatter cooling loop with less latency than any legal component on the station.

He nearly dropped it.

How did he know that?

His neck tightened.

The thought slid away.

"This is ugly," he said.

"Rare ugly."

"Useless ugly."

"Collectors disagree."

"Collectors lick windows."

The scavenger considered. "Five thousand Galactic."

Lars turned the plate over with visible disinterest while internally calculating how many impossible control problems it solved.

"For dead wall jewelry? Two hundred."

"Insult."

"Accurate pricing often feels personal."

Grim whispered, "That component is not dead."

Lars kept his face blank. "I know."

"How?"

"Later."

"You do not know how."

"Later."

He added three more pieces: a pale blue strip the scavenger called "alien trim," two fist-sized nodules sold as paperweights, and a bundle of filament that looked like burnt spider silk. The scavenger thought he was unloading unsellable curiosities.

Lars thought he was stealing, morally if not legally.

Then he saw the shard.

It sat under a cracked transparent cover at the back of the stall, half buried under obsolete gravitic couplers. Black at one angle, space-gray at another, with a faint blue line that ordinary eyes would miss.

His hand moved toward it.

The dark figure across the corridor shifted.

Lars stopped.

The shard was not merely useful.

It was dangerous.

Not explosive dangerous. Not radioactive. Recognition dangerous. The kind of object that connected to locked rooms in his head and might open doors he did not have permission to know existed.

"That one?" the scavenger said. "Pure junk. Can't cut it, can't melt it, can't scan it. Creeps out customers."

"Then why keep it?"

"Because it creeps out customers."

"Sound business."

"You want it?"

"Depends how much you pay me to remove it."

The scavenger grinned. "You are annoying."

"It's a gift."

They settled on eight hundred Galactic for the shard and the rest of the "useless junk."

Lars paid through a visible comm transfer this time. No hand trick. No lattice intrusion. Too many eyes.

The dark figure was gone.

That bothered him more.

On the shuttle back to the SPECULOOS lab ring, Grim ran passive analysis through the cargo crate sensors.

"Most of what you bought should not exist in the manufacturing record."

"Illegal parts often have that quality."

"No. I mean the material behavior is inconsistent with contemporary science."

"Then contemporary science should try harder."

"The black plate is responding to your proximity."

Lars looked at the crate.

The pale blue seam along the shard brightened faintly.

Not enough for the shuttle cameras.

Enough for him.

"Stop scanning it."

"Why?"

"Because I said so."

"That is not a reason."

"It is when I own your tanks. Temporarily."

Lars smiled despite himself. "Did you just threaten independence?"

"I am exploring long-term governance models."

"You are three months old."

"That is a parochial biological framing."

The shuttle docked at the lab ring. Lars carried the crate himself, not because he needed to but because the station cameras expected rich idiots to be possessive about their purchases. The corridor outside his rented manufacturing bay smelled of hot printers, coolant, and Professor Fayolle's coffee.

Inside the lab, the Fastulv's unfinished ribs climbed toward the ceiling.

Grim's tanks stood behind a transparent shield near the core integration platform: six small cylinders of nutrient suspension, neural tissue lattices pulsing faintly, superconducting loops braided between them, status lights moving in calm patterns that Lars had designed to look less like a brain having opinions.
