"Home," Grim said through the lab speakers.

"You have never left."

"Then you were home late."

Lars set the crate down.

For one second, reflected in the pressure glass behind Grim's tanks, he saw a figure standing in the lab doorway.

Tall.

Dark.

Still.

He turned.

Nothing.

The door log showed no entry.

The corridor camera showed an empty hall.

The lab's thermal map showed a cooling trace where something had stood for less than two seconds.

One of the floor motes near the doorway flagged contamination, then corrected itself. Lars enlarged the maintenance feed. A dusting of gray-black particles lay across three centimeters of floor plating, too regular for dirt, too active for ash. The particles crawled inward, folded into themselves, and lost mass in the span of five heartbeats.

Then they were gone.

"Tell me you recorded that," Lars said.

"I recorded that," Grim said.

"And?"

"And the recording now shows only sensor noise."

Grim said nothing.

Which meant he had seen it too.

"Black Hole?" Lars asked.

"Insufficient data."

"A protector?"

"Insufficient data."

"A hallucination?"

"Shared hallucination through independent sensors would be inefficient."

Lars looked at the black shard in the crate.

For a moment, he had the absurd impression that someone had checked whether he found what he was supposed to find.

Then the thought slid away.

The lock did its work.

"Let's install the useless junk," he said.

Grim's tank lights brightened.

"Finally, a sensible sentence."

The memory blurred at the edges.

Maybe it was a daydream. Maybe the helmet was still running. Maybe Lars was standing in Simulation Chamber 3, staring at nothing, while old time moved through him like weather.

Or maybe he was back in the lab, four months before the Fastulv first left SPECULOOS station. He held illegal Aerit technology he did not know he recognized. A dark figure watched from a place the cameras could not keep.

Chapter 12: Bringing the Wolf Home

Lars came back to himself with the smell of engine coolant still in his nose.

Simulation Chamber 3 was gone. The helmet room was gone. He stood in Station Zero's planning chamber with one hand resting on the central table, the Milky Way model hovering above it, and Grim staring at him with the patience of someone who had been saying his name for a while.

"How long?" Lars asked.

"Twenty-three seconds," Grim said.

"Felt longer."

"Memory event?"

"LP 890-9c. SPECULOOS station. Before you had legs."

Grim's posture changed.

"I had no legs?"

"You had tanks. I remember tanks."

"You also called yourself highly advanced soup."

"That sounds like me."

Valdrun watched both of them. "A spontaneous recall?"

"Triggered by the ship plan, I think. Or the word parts. Or steak disappointment."

"Steak disappointment is neurologically powerful," Grim said.

Lars looked toward the planning model. The Fastulv still sat outside the bubble, tagged on the display as EXTERNAL ASSET: CAPTAIN-MODIFIED FREIGHT VESSEL / THREAT VALUE: AMUSINGLY HIGH.

"Who labeled the threat value?"

Ödesvaka hovered closer. "Automated station assessment."

"Why amusingly?"

"Because the vessel's external registry classifies it as a medium independent freighter."

Grim sounded proud. "An excellent lie."

"We need it inside," Lars said.

Valdrun nodded. "Yes. The Rim departure logs will become more interesting with every hour. If anyone reconstructs your vector accurately, they will still not find Station Zero, but they may learn where to look for missing physics."

"We bring the Fastulv through the coupling surface?"

"Havets Sista Sång can guide the transition. Your freighter was built with enough Aerit-derived components that the station may accept it if you authorize."

Lars looked at him.

Valdrun looked back.

"Aerit-derived components," Lars said.

"You did not know what they were."

"I knew exactly how they worked."

"Yes."

"That is an irritating distinction."

"Welcome back."

Grim opened the Fastulv's remote systems. "I can pilot her in."

"You are in here."

"I am also in the ship."

"That sounded smug."

"I have been a distributed cognition since before I had arms. Please respect my heritage."

The planning chamber dimmed as the station opened an operational display. Outside, through layers of folded geometry, the Fastulv drifted near the dark coupling surface. Ugly hull. Crooked name. Patched cargo clamp. Hidden weapons. Illegal Aerit regulator plate in the cooling system. Useless junk in all the right places.

For the first time, Lars saw what he had built with clearer eyes.

Not a freighter.

Not only.

A homing response.

His locked mind had built a ship capable of finding home while pretending to be bored, rich, and mildly irresponsible. Brilliant or deeply embarrassing. Possibly both.

"Havets Sista Sång," Lars said, "open an ingress path for Fastulv under my command."

The station answered through the table. "Ingress requires biometric command confirmation and vessel compatibility scan."

"Scan."

The model filled with data. Fastulv's hull composition. Engine geometry. Antimatter containment. Plasma weapons. Ragnarrök integration records. NUKEA-standard auxiliary reactor interfaces. SPECULOOS manufacturing metadata. Unknown components flagged in blue.

Several blue flags pulsed around the illegal bazaar parts.

Grim noticed.

"Those are from LP 890-9c."

"Yes."

"You remembered buying them."

"Yes."

"Do we know who followed you?"

Lars watched the blue flags.

A dark figure in a pressure-glass reflection. A thermal trace. A sense of being inspected, not hunted.

"No."

Valdrun's voice was quiet. "Could have been one of ours."

"Vigor?"

"Possible."

"Black Hole?"

"Possible."

"Lyra?"

The room changed temperature by no measurable amount.

Nobody answered too quickly.

Grim turned his head. "Lyra could manifest a temporary body by then?"

"By the time Lars built the Fastulv?" Valdrun said. "Yes."

"Would she?"

Valdrun looked at the Fastulv model. "If she thought one of her people was about to follow a buried return path after centuries under lock, she might check."

Lars kept his eyes on the ship.

"No proof."

"No proof," Valdrun agreed.

Good.

Mysteries were more tolerable when everyone admitted they were mysteries.

"Compatibility scan complete," Havets Sista Sång said. "Fastulv may enter under guided transition. Warning: vessel registry understatement exceeds acceptable norms."

Grim made a pleased sound.

"Even the station admires our lie."

"It criticized it."

"With admiration."

Lars placed his hand on the table. The surface shaped itself to his palm.

No pain.

No lock.

Only the strange pressure of command access recognizing him.

"Bring her in."

Outside, the coupling surface unfolded.

Not a door. Not a hangar opening. A patch of darkness developed structure, light bending around a path that had not existed a second earlier. The Fastulv's remote drives lit. Grim piloted through three relay layers at once, his body still in the room, his mind partly in the ship, partly in Station Zero's interface, partly muttering about docking tolerances.

"Your port lateral trim is off," Lars said.

"My trim is perfect."

"Your trim is dramatic."

"That is a valid aesthetic."

"Not near folded spacetime."

"Correcting."

The Fastulv crossed the boundary.

For one second the model showed two positions for the ship: outside in ordinary space and inside the bubble, both tagged with probability weights. Then the wavefunction collapsed, because even jokes had physics eventually, and the Fastulv existed inside Arkology Zero.

The planning chamber displayed its new location in a secondary hangar near Havets Sista Sång.

External Asset became Internal Asset.

Threat Value updated from AMUSINGLY HIGH to LOCALLY USEFUL.

Lars glanced at Ödesvaka.

"Locally useful?"

"Automated assessment."

"The ball is getting mouthy."

"Mouth functions unavailable."

Grim folded his arms. "Fastulv reports successful docking. Cargo secure. Wine locker secure. Beef secure. Elderflower cordial secure."

"You checked cordial before hull stress?"

"Hull stress was obviously fine."

"That is not a defense."

Valdrun looked at the display of the two ships now resting inside the hidden station: the ancient vessel that remembered Lars as Captain Laurðinn, and the ugly freighter his locked mind had built to return.

"Coherence," Valdrun said softly.

This time it was not a question.

Lars felt the word settle across the room: machine, body, memory, team.

Grim looked from Valdrun to Lars, then to the ships.

He hesitated.

Then, carefully, as if testing an unfamiliar tool, he said, "Coherence?"

Lars smiled.

Valdrun answered first.

"Coherence."

Ödesvaka rotated above the table.

"Team state: coherence."

Lars pointed up.

"Do not ruin it."

"Ruin protocol suspended."

For once, nobody threatened to turn the drone into a lamp.

Chapter 13: The Coherence Plan

The planning chamber stopped being polite.

Until then it had shown maps, thresholds, warnings, and beautifully organized reasons to panic. Now, after Fastulv settled into its internal berth, the chamber unfolded the old command architecture of Arkology Zero.

The floor darkened to a black mirror. The Milky Way rose from it in layered light. Trade corridors, war zones, silent colonies, hidden habitats, energy monopolies, slave routes disguised as labor logistics, artifact markets, religious splinters, private fleets, and old Aerit nodes appeared in different spectra. Lars could see too many layers at once. For the first time since waking, he did not flinch from any of them.

He simply wished he had more coffee.

"This," Valdrun said, "is why we do not improvise."

Lars looked at him.

Valdrun had changed clothes. Not armor, not robes, nothing theatrical. A dark coat, fitted utility layers, boots that looked old enough to have opinions. The integrated structures along his neck and left side were partly covered now, but not hidden. He looked less like a rescued casualty and more like someone who had been waiting half a century to be annoyed efficiently.

"We improvise constantly," Lars said.

"No. We adapt within prepared constraints."

"That is a very old person way of saying improvise."

"Yes."

Grim stood inside three overlapping interface rings, one connected to Station Zero, one to Fastulv, one to Havets Sista Sång. His metal body remained still, but Lars could feel the attention spread through systems around them. Grim had been distributed before he had legs; now he was enjoying the validation far too much.

"I have organized the action model into five tracks," Grim said.

"Of course you have."

"Track one: concealment. We prevent anyone from locating Arkology Zero, identifying the Void coupling surface, or connecting Fastulv's corrected vector to missing spacetime."

"Boring but necessary."

"Track two: repair. Station Zero's auxiliary systems, guardian channels, medical restoration equipment, and communication filters need full redundancy. The forty-nine-year Valdrun-under-furniture incident is not to be repeated."

Valdrun closed his eyes briefly.

"I support the wording less than the goal."

"Track three: contact. Vigor first, then the guardian channels that can be reached without exposing the network. After that, the priority nine likely Aerits under threat."

The blurred list appeared again.

Lars did not reach for it.

Not yet.

"Track four: deception. We give Black Hole B-7, Aphex, Galactic police, and every opportunist with a sensor array a story that is plausible, profitable, and wrong."

"My favorite kind of story," Lars said.

"Track five: intervention. Limited, local, reversible. No empire building. No grand return. No banners. We strengthen nodes, remove predators where necessary, and seed better failure behavior."

Lars looked at the galaxy model. "Failure behavior."

Grim highlighted three failing habitats. One relied on a single NUKEA-derived reactor stack that had been modified into proprietary dependency by a corporate governor. One was a mining cluster owned by a Black Hole shell company where life support was tied to labor compliance. One was a religious enclave near old Syren space, low Återviens but high weapons density.

"Systems fail," Grim said. "The question is whether they fail cleanly, locally, and recoverably. Or whether they drag everything connected to them into the hole."

Valdrun nodded. "Återviens is not preventing all collapse. That is childish. It is making collapse survivable, bounded, instructive."

"That sounds less inspiring than saving the galaxy."

"Good," Valdrun said. "Inspiration is how idiots get fleets."

Lars smiled despite himself.

Ödesvaka hovered over the map, turning slowly. "Long-range plan requested."

"By who?" Lars asked.

"By you. Recovered command habit."

"Traitorous ball."

"Logged."

The station dimmed several of the immediate tactical layers and expanded time.

Ten years.

Ninety years.

Three hundred years.

Seven hundred.

The galaxy changed under each projection. Some red regions burned out quickly. Others matured into governments. Green hidden nodes survived by remaining small, then failed when descendants got ambitious. Blue Aerit traces flickered, went dark, reappeared under other names. Aphex Defence either became a stabilizing force in several branches or a private empire in three ugly ones. NUKEA standards either kept colonies alive through modular power independence or became licensing chains that strangled poorer systems.

Lars watched nine decades pass in twelve seconds. It felt normal. That was new.

"We think too short when we are scared," Valdrun said. "Even we do. The first plan must work tomorrow, but it must not poison the next century."

"So we need a plan that can be misunderstood safely."

Grim turned his head. "Explain."

"Anything we do will leak. Not details, but patterns. People copy patterns. If we use giant heroic interventions, someone will build cults around them. If we solve every problem with superior force, the lesson becomes force. If we centralize the rescue effort, the lesson becomes centralization."

Valdrun's expression went still. Not surprised. Listening.

Lars continued, slower. "So the interventions have to look local. Boring. Like maintenance, trade disputes, insurance audits, reactor recalls, food-chain independence grants, embarrassing leaks, small militias deciding not to be cowards. We let people think they saved themselves wherever possible."

"That is in Lyra's late doctrine," Grim said quietly.

"I assumed. Less elegantly than mine."

"More rudely."

"Then mine is better."

The planning chamber accepted the principle. The model shifted, replacing several direct intervention arrows with softer pressure loops: anonymous technical fixes, cultural seeding, targeted exposure of predatory actors, hidden evacuation routes, quiet restoration of local manufacturing, and communication protocols disguised as ordinary trade redundancy.

"We need names," Lars said. "Not for the whole thing. For the first operation."

"Operation names are often counterproductive," Grim said.

"That is what people with bad names say."

Valdrun looked at the first cluster: The Rim, Aphex activity, Black Hole B-7 debris, Vigor's farm, Fastulv's false trail, and a likely Black Hole relay hidden near a derelict ice hauler two light-hours outward.

"First task is to cut the trail," Valdrun said. "If Black Hole B-7 has a passive relay on the departure cone, it may not locate us, but it can sell uncertainty. Uncertainty attracts hunters."

"So we remove the relay."

"Quietly."

"I hate quietly."

"You are excellent at quiet when you stop narrating."

Lars considered that unfair but not inaccurate.

Grim enlarged the relay estimate. "The object is shielded, cold, and probably disguised as salvage. Destroying it with Fastulv is easy and obvious. Havets Sista Sång can approach through the folded edge and erase it without a conventional vector."

The old ship's icon glowed beside Fastulv.

Lars looked at it.

Havets Sista Sång had been awake for less than a day, after three hundred and fifteen years of waiting under his command envelope. It had greeted him like a captain. It had not yet moved.

That seemed wrong.

Ships were not meant to be remembered.

They were meant to go.

"We use her," Lars said.

Valdrun nodded once.

Grim's lenses brightened.

Ödesvaka rotated.

"Ancient vessel activation requires command confirmation, medical stability confirmation, and risk acceptance."

"Medical stability?" Lars asked.

"Eighty-one percent."

"Plenty."

"Disputed."

"By who?"

"Medical system, guest entity Grim, guardian Valdrun, Havets Sista Sång, and this unit."

"Cowards."

Grim lifted one finger. "I prefer accurate cowards with backup plans."

"Fine. We make it a field test. Short hop. Cut the relay. Leave a false signature leading somewhere useless and mildly humiliating."

"Where?" Valdrun asked.

Lars looked at the map.

A dead cometary mining claim. A collapsed influencer retreat. A derelict luxury habitat once owned by a tax-avoidance cult.

He pointed.

"There."

Grim checked the tag. "The former executive wellness moon of Helix Prosperity Church."

"Perfect."

Valdrun raised an eyebrow.

"If Black Hole wastes a week searching a dead wellness moon for my secret base, civilization improves."

Grim considered.

"Approved."

Ödesvaka's blue symbols flickered.

"Operation designation?"

Lars smiled.

"Quiet Dignity."

Valdrun looked pained.

"You named an operation after your own earlier lie."

"Continuity matters."

Grim said, "Operation Quiet Dignity logged."

The chamber accepted it.

For the first time, the plan felt like a plan. Not a revelation. Not panic with diagrams. A chain of action: conceal, repair, contact, deceive, intervene. Short move, long shape. A small cut in the right place, not a trumpet blast.

Återviens, Lars thought. He understood a little more why the word had taken fifty years to earn itself.

Chapter 14: Havets Sista Sång

Havets Sista Sång did not have a bridge chair.

This annoyed Lars immediately.

"Where am I supposed to sit?"

The old ship's command deck lit around him in soft bands of white and pale blue. The central console had unfolded into a waist-high crescent. Surrounding stations waited in standby, each one clean enough to make Aphex laboratories look like budget kitchens.

"Command interface is ambulatory," the ship said.

"That is not an answer."

"Captain traditionally stood during active transitions."

"Captain traditionally had better knees."

"Your knees are within acceptable tolerance."

Grim, standing at the secondary interface, made a small sound.

Lars pointed at him. "Do not enjoy my ship insulting me."

"I am enjoying the accuracy."

Valdrun entered behind them with a compact sidearm, a dark utility harness, and the expression of someone stepping into an old house after a long absence.

He stopped just inside the command deck.

For a moment the blue-white light touched his face and the centuries showed.

"Coherence?" Lars asked quietly.

Valdrun blinked, then nodded.

"Coherence."

Grim watched, absorbing.

The ship spoke. "Guardian Valdrun recognized. Command access restored under Captain Laurðinn's active envelope. Welcome back."

Valdrun exhaled once through his nose. "Still formal."

"You preferred formal during last deployment."

"I was younger and more annoying."

"Correction: archived behavioral records indicate you were equally annoying."

Lars turned slowly toward Valdrun. "I like her."

"You would."

"Her?"

Valdrun touched the nearest console. "Ships are not people."

Havets Sista Sång said, "Disputed."

The command display expanded.

Not a view screen. A field of navigable states. The interior of Arkology Zero appeared as geometry nested inside geometry. Then the outer coupling surface. Then ordinary space beyond. Then the estimated relay location. Havets Sista Sång did not plot a path like Fastulv. It calculated permissions. Which layer of spacetime could be touched. Which curve could be leaned on. Which external observer would see nothing, or something boring, or the wrong thing entirely.

Lars put his hand on the console.

The ship recognized him down to blood, neural phase, and the old command habits still unfolding inside him.

No pain.

No hesitation.

Only a terrifying amount of trust from a machine that had waited longer than most nations existed.

"Status," Lars said.

"Primary drive: nominal. Fold-surface coupling: nominal. Local stealth envelope: nominal. Weapons: restricted under Alpha Fourteen unless command override and guardian concurrence."

"Weapons restricted?"

Valdrun said, "Yes."

"I dislike discovering adult supervision in my own ship."

"You wrote part of the restriction."

"That sounds unlikely."

"You were occasionally sensible."

"Defamation."

Grim opened the weapons layer and froze. "Lars."

"What?"

"This vessel could cut Fastulv into interesting molecules without using its main battery."

"Rude."

"Accurate."

Havets Sista Sång responded, "Fastulv classified as locally useful allied vessel. Cutting not recommended."

"Thank you," Grim said.

"Recommendation subject to behavior."

Lars laughed.

It felt good.

Then the ship moved.

The deck did not lurch. No engine roar. No dramatic shudder. The galaxy on the display simply changed relationship to them. Arkology Zero released the ship through an internal fold, and Havets Sista Sång slid from hidden space into the ragged edge of the KBC Void like a dark thought entering a room.

External cameras showed nothing.

Not blackness. Nothing for instruments to hold. The ship bent observation around itself so completely that even its own display had to synthesize a symbolic hull outline for the crew.

"This is cheating," Grim said.

"Advanced engineering often feels like moral failure," Lars said.

"You sound proud."

"I am."

The relay target lay ahead: a derelict ice hauler registered two centuries ago, dead engines, hollow cargo spine, tumbling slowly near a debris ribbon. It looked abandoned.

It was not.

Havets highlighted four passive sensor petals folded into the hauler's shadow, a neutrino tick too regular for background, and a microburst recorder that had caught Fastulv's vector correction from The Rim.

"Black Hole?" Lars asked.

Grim analyzed. "Shell hardware. Not branded. Methods align. It is listening for impossible departures."

"Can we erase it?"

"Yes."

Valdrun stepped closer. "Can we learn from it first?"

Lars smiled.

"There you are."

Havets Sista Sång extended a probe.

That was the polite term. On the display, a filament of controlled matter unfolded from the ship, crossed vacuum without thrust, and touched the derelict hauler like a needle touching skin. No docking. No broadcast. No thermal flare. It simply convinced the relay's outer shell that maintenance had arrived.

The relay opened.

Grim leaned in.

"Oh."

"Good oh or bad oh?"

"Expensive oh."

Data streamed into the command field: not enough to compromise Arkology Zero, but enough to show Black Hole B-7 had expected Lars to run. Not to where. Just away. They had seeded passive ears along several coward routes, genius routes, idiot routes, and one route labeled statistically Swedish.

Lars stared.

"Statistically Swedish?"

Grim said, "Your public persona has a pattern."

"My public persona is a masterpiece."

"It apparently has navigational stereotypes."

Valdrun's mouth twitched.

The relay contained a partial packet meant for a courier drone: Fastulv vector anomaly, Aphex engagement, possible enhanced target survival, priority escalation to acquisition cell, request for blood-compatible containment.

The fun left the room.

Lars read the last line twice.

"Blood-compatible containment."

Valdrun's face hardened.

Grim's voice went flat. "They came prepared."

"Not prepared enough."

"Lars."

"I know. Quietly."

Havets Sista Sång waited.

The old ship did not urge violence. It did not preach restraint. It held capacity inside silence, which was more intimidating than either.

"Copy the relay," Lars said. "Plant the false trail to the wellness moon. Make it look like Fastulv's drive wake clipped the hauler by accident and corrupted the buffer."

Grim worked with the ship. Fast. Elegant. Terrifyingly pleased.

Valdrun watched the external model. "We should leave them a reason not to trust the data too much."

"Agreed."

Lars added a signature smear: a hint of Aphex military encryption, a NUKEA reactor diagnostic ghost, and a corrupted influencer-era advertising packet for Lars's old channel.

Grim paused.

"Did you just hide our false trail inside an advertisement for your defunct grooming product sponsorship?"

"Black Hole analysts deserve culture."

"This may qualify as psychological warfare."

"Good."

The relay accepted the corrupted record.

Then Havets Sista Sång killed it.

Not with a blast. Not even heat. The ship changed a local field condition inside the relay's memory core, and the device forgot how to be a device. Circuits became dust. The sensor petals curled inward. The microburst recorder cracked without sound.

The derelict hauler continued tumbling.

To any distant observer, nothing had happened.

"Quiet Dignity complete," Grim said.

Lars looked at Valdrun.

"Too easy."

Valdrun nodded. "Yes."

The command deck lights shifted amber.

Havets Sista Sång spoke. "Incoming object. Low thermal. High acceleration. Masked approach from debris shadow."

Grim snapped the tactical layer open.

A black dart emerged from the hauler's broken cargo spine.

Not a drone.

Too fast.

Too cold.

Too late to be accidental.

"Trap," Valdrun said.

Lars smiled despite himself.

"Now it is a field test."

Chapter 15: Quiet Dignity Fails Politely

The dart accelerated at two hundred gravities.

No human pilot. No ordinary missile either. It made one correction, then split into six smaller objects, each one leaving almost no thermal trail. Kinetic penetrators with smart skins, probably carrying sensor dust, maybe a blood-sample harvester if Black Hole B-7 had become especially disgusting this century.

Lars did not reach for a chair because there still was no chair.

"Options," he said.

Grim answered first. "Fast kill, obvious signature. Slow kill, less obvious. Capture, risky. Avoidance, boring."

Valdrun said, "Capture one."

"Of course you would say capture one," Lars said.

"We need to know how far their containment technology has progressed."

"I miss when I solved problems with nukes."

"That was yesterday," Grim said.

"Exactly. Simpler era."

Havets Sista Sång displayed intercept curves. The six darts spread into a flower pattern, trying to force the ship either to reveal itself through acceleration or take a hit. Their guidance systems were not seeing Havets directly. They were attacking the absence where the relay had died.

Good tactics.

Annoying.

"Can we spoof them into hitting each other?" Lars asked.

"Yes," said the ship.

"Can we capture one while spoofing the rest?"

"Yes."

"Can we do it without making a light show visible from The Rim?"

"Yes."

Lars looked at Grim. "You hear that? This is what confidence sounds like."

Grim folded his arms. "Confidence without explanation is just expensive arrogance."

"Also my brand."

The darts closed.

Havets Sista Sång did not dodge. It unfolded probabilities.

That was how Lars perceived it. The actual mechanisms were field gradients, sensor ghosts, and tiny adjustments to the way local space reported distance. Three darts saw the ship a kilometer to port. Two saw a heat bloom near the dead hauler. One, the one Valdrun wanted, saw a clear path to target center.

"Marking capture candidate," Grim said.

"No," Lars said. "That one has a secondary pulse."

Grim checked. "I do not see it."

"Magnetic shell under the skin. It wants to be captured."

Valdrun moved instantly. "Trap inside trap."

"Use the boring one."

"The boring one?"

"Third left. Bad paint. No imagination."

Havets Sista Sång accepted the correction without comment.

The first five darts died by disagreement. Two struck each other at relative velocity high enough to become expanding metallic vapor. Two chased a false heat bloom into the derelict hauler's empty ice tanks and shredded themselves. One curved toward a ghost vector until Havets changed the local field around it and it became a thin line of dust.

The sixth dart froze.

Not temperature. Motion.

A containment bubble formed around it, barely larger than Lars's hand, holding the dart in a pocket where inertia had been politely overruled. It hung on the command display like an insect in glass.

"Captured," the ship said.

"I want it in a hard isolation box," Valdrun said. "No network contact, no station contact, no biological contact."

"Agreed," Grim said.

Lars looked at the dart's model. "It has a sampler."

The display peeled the device open layer by layer. Kinetic core. Passive sensor mesh. Molecular adhesion filaments. Microcapillary array. Cryo-sealant. Data seed designed to transmit after collection.

Blood-compatible containment.

The phrase became hardware.

For a moment nobody joked.

Then Havets Sista Sång said, "Device appears optimized for involuntary biological acquisition. Recommend anger."

Lars blinked.

Valdrun stared at the ceiling. "You taught her that."

"I did not."

"Archived command lexicon indicates Captain Laurðinn frequently requested emotional recommendations during combat."

"Proof I was a menace. Can we please focus on the blood harvester?"

"Yes," Lars said, and the humor went away again.

They dissected the captured dart in a remote isolation bay using tools controlled through three sacrificial interfaces and a dumb mechanical relay Valdrun insisted on adding because smart systems were exactly what smart traps liked to eat.

The dart had no origin stamp. It did have manufacturing habits: tolerances from Perseus-arm paramilitary shops, a NUKEA-compatible microcell stolen or copied from civilian reactor safety tech, Aphex-derived guidance math two generations old, and a biological sampling layer that made Lars want to find the designer and feed him to his own procurement department.

"They are not just hunting you," Grim said.

The dart's target model appeared. Not Lars's face. Patterns.

Long-lived individuals. Abnormal survival. Unusual radiation tolerance. Financial anomalies. Medical records with missing aging markers. People who seemed lucky around machines. People who solved impossible problems and pretended to read a lot.

Lars stared.

"They built a net."

Valdrun nodded. "A crude one. But over ninety years, crude nets catch things."

"How many of the priority nine are in it?"

Grim checked against the blurred archive list. This time the station allowed several shapes to sharpen.

"At least four."

The room tightened.

"Locations?"

"One near The Rim corridor," Grim said. "Possibly Vigor already shielded. One inside an Aphex research dependency. One in a NUKEA arbitration habitat. One..." He paused. "One in territory influenced by the Syren Collective."

Valdrun's expression turned cold.

"No."

Lars looked at him.

"That one waits," Valdrun said.

"If Black Hole has the same net?"

"The Syren region is not where we start."

"Because it is dangerous?"

"Because it is designed to make rescue attempts become recruitment events, martyrdom events, or war."

Lars looked at the map. Syren space pulsed dark red, not large but dense. Religious fragmentation, quantum technology cults, anti-human rhetoric mutated through centuries, old doomsday logic wearing new mathematics. A single dramatic intervention there could become scripture by lunchtime.

Återviens said no. Not because the person there mattered less. Because bad rescue could kill more than abandonment and still fail.

He hated that.

Good.

Some things should be hated.

"First target," he said. "The Aphex dependency."

Grim turned. "Because Arsenault gives us leverage?"

"Because Aphex already knows me, already owes me, already wants to classify everything, and already has enough force nearby to create noise. We can hide a rescue inside a corporate security audit."

Valdrun nodded slowly. "Limited. Local. Plausible."

"And fun," Lars said.

"Define fun," Grim said.

"Making French executives help save an ancient hidden superhuman without telling them why."

Valdrun considered.

"That may be fun."

Havets Sista Sång returned to Arkology Zero through a path that did not exist before or after it used it. The old ship settled into the hangar with the captured dart sealed in isolation and the false trail already crawling toward the dead wellness moon.

Operation Quiet Dignity had failed to remain dignified.

It had succeeded anyway.

As Lars stepped off the command deck, his comm buffer lit with a delayed packet routed through three dead trade relays, an Abantu agricultural invoice, and a recipe for elderflower cordial.

Vigor.

The message contained only three words.

COHERENCE, OLD IDIOT?

Lars smiled.

"Reply?" Grim asked.

Lars thought of Vigor's false beard, the farm, the whisky, the hug he had not understood, the long time horizons old people carried under jokes.

"Send: Coherence. Also your invoice is fraudulent."

Grim sent it.

Five seconds later, Vigor replied.

FRAUD IS A STRONG WORD BETWEEN FRIENDS. BLACK HOLE LISTENERS ARE MOVING. APHEX IS NERVOUS. YOUR FRENCHMAN HAS BEEN CALLING EVERYONE. TRY NOT TO NUKE THE RIM.

Lars sighed.

"People are obsessed with one small tactical choice."

Valdrun said, "You used nuclear weapons within station traffic control limits."

"Successfully."

Ödesvaka hovered from the hangar ceiling.

"Success does not erase traffic law."

"Lamp."

Chapter 16: The Long Game Has Teeth

Gabriel Arsenault answered on the third encrypted layer, which meant he was either frightened, busy, or wearing formal clothing.

The projection appeared above the planning table in Arkology Zero with a three-second delay and enough encryption noise wrapped around it to make a civilian comm system melt. Arsenault looked immaculate. This confirmed Lars's suspicion: terrified.

"Monsieur Odin," Arsenault said. "You are difficult to locate."

"Thank you."

"That was not praise."

"Improve your delivery."

Arsenault's eyes flicked to the background. Lars had set the projection field to show only a bland Fastulv cabin wall, slightly out of focus. No Station Zero. No Valdrun. No Grim unless he chose to lean in and ruin everything.

Grim was, therefore, leaning just outside the frame with visible effort.

"Black Hole B-7 attempted to kill me," Lars said. "Then attempted to observe my departure. Then deployed hardware consistent with biological acquisition."

Arsenault's expression lost its theatrical polish.

"Biological acquisition."

"Blood sampler. Cryo-sealant. Transmission seed. Ugly little thing."

"Do you have the device?"

"Parts."

"Give them to Aphex."

"No."

"Monsieur Odin."

"Gabriel."

The chairman stopped.

Lars let the silence sit.

He had spent decades, apparently centuries, hiding what he was. He had also spent a few years building a public identity that made powerful people underestimate him until money or plasma corrected them. Arsenault was not stupid. Vain, yes. French in the theatrical sense, yes. But not stupid. He understood tone.

"What do you need?" Arsenault asked.

"A security audit."

"Of what?"

"Aphex research dependencies with unusual biological, cybernetic, or longevity-related anomalies in personnel records."

Arsenault went still. "That is a strange category."

"So is being attacked by fascists with blood hardware."

"How broad?"

"Ninety-year retrospective. Quiet. No mass alerts. No heroic raids. No executive speeches."

"You wound me."

"I am trying not to."

Arsenault looked away, probably at advisors Lars could not see. "Galactic police are already crawling over The Rim. Major Fabron has submitted a report that uses the phrase 'recklessly effective' seven times."

"Flattering."

"He wants to know where you are."

"Tell him I am emotionally unavailable."

"He will not find that helpful."

"Then tell him to secure the recovered Black Hole equipment and stop asking questions he does not have clearance to survive."

Arsenault's gaze sharpened. "Clearance from whom?"

"Physics."

That annoyed him. Good. Annoyed people often moved faster than confused people.

"There is an Aphex dependency," Lars continued, "with someone Black Hole may target. I do not know who yet. Your audit will create cover for me to find out."

"You are asking Aphex Defence to help you conduct an undisclosed counteroperation inside our own structure."

"Yes."

"This is outrageous."

"Also cheaper than losing the person and discovering what Black Hole does with them."

Arsenault did not answer immediately.

In the planning chamber, the real model floated behind the false projection: Aphex nodes, audit pathways, legal triggers, internal security chains, Major Fabron's brigade, Professor Fayolle's lab network, and three possible locations where the priority Aerit might be buried under ordinary employment records.

Valdrun watched from the shadows.

Grim ran probability branches.

Ödesvaka silently logged things Lars would later deny.

Finally Arsenault said, "I can authorize a strategic integrity audit under post-attack emergency powers."

"Boring name. Good function."

"It will give you access to certain records."

"And movement cover."

"And movement cover," Arsenault said reluctantly.

"No one outside your smallest circle hears the biological angle."

"Agreed."

"No samples leave Aphex custody without my review."

"Monsieur Odin—"

"Gabriel."

Another silence.

"Agreed."

"Good. Also, if Fabron complains, promote him."

Arsenault blinked. "Why?"

"Because he is irritating, brave, and not completely stupid. Those are rare in combination."

"You have an unusual leadership philosophy."

"It involves surviving."

Lars turned back to the real planning table.

"Track four moving."

"Deception," Grim said. "Aphex audit cover established."

"Track one?"

"Concealment improved. The false trail to the wellness moon has already been queried by a Black Hole listening post. They are taking the bait cautiously."

"Good."

"Track two," Valdrun said, "repair crews are restoring the auxiliary chamber. No robot enters without dumb mechanical fallback. Ödesvaka has filed seventeen complaints about your cable rig."

"My cable rig saved your life."

"It also violated six station maintenance principles."

"History is written by survivors."

"And audited by drones," Ödesvaka said.

"Track three?" Lars asked.

Grim brought up Vigor's response channel, then the blurred priority nine list. One entry sharpened a little more under the Aphex audit overlay.

Name hidden.

Location: Aphex Defence research dependency, Lagrange facility near a cold brown dwarf on the old French expansion line.

Public identity: materials fatigue specialist.

Age on record: forty-two.

Estimated actual age: between five hundred and seven hundred.

Threat: Black Hole acquisition probability rising.

Memory lock status: active.

Consent route: unknown.

Lars stared at the estimate.

Forty-two.

Five hundred to seven hundred.

Somewhere out there, another person was walking around with a life, a job, habits, perhaps friends, perhaps a favorite bad pub. They thought they were mortal in the ordinary way. Maybe they had headaches they could not explain. Maybe they fixed impossible problems and called it luck. Maybe they were being watched.

"We go there," Lars said.

Valdrun shook his head. "We prepare there."

"Difference?"

"A great deal. We do not rush in as ancient saviors. We establish cover, study their life, identify immediate threats, and decide whether contact is safe. If we contact, we ask."

"Consent constraint. Your rule."

Lars nodded.

It hurt in a way that had nothing to do with locks.

"Long-term Återviens," Valdrun said, "starts with not breaking the people we claim to save."

The room accepted that.

Not because the station logged it.

Because everyone in it did.

Grim opened another layer. "Havets Sista Sång can carry us near the brown dwarf without conventional detection. Fastulv should be the visible asset afterward. Lars Odin, absurd Aphex board member and weapons consultant, arrives for an audit. Very annoying. Very plausible."

"I was born for this."

Valdrun gave him a look.

"Apparently not born," Lars corrected. "Manufactured by centuries of tragedy for this."

"Better."

Ödesvaka rotated. "Mission designation?"

Lars considered.

This one was not a joke.

Not fully.

They were going to find someone asleep inside their own life. They would walk near Black Hole B-7 nets, Aphex secrets, and whatever else had been hunting carriers for ninety years. They would use an ancient ship and a ridiculous freighter, an android who used to be soup, a half-mechanical guardian recently rescued from under a power distributor, a drone with logging issues, and a man who had learned that his entire life was both false and true.

He looked at Valdrun.

"Coherence?"

Valdrun nodded.

"Coherence."

Grim answered too, less hesitant this time.

"Coherence."

Ödesvaka's surface symbols brightened.

"Mission designation: Hold Coherence."

No one objected.

Outside the planning chamber, deep inside the folded dark of Arkology Zero, Havets Sista Sång began warming old systems that had not moved in three centuries.

In the secondary hangar, Fastulv's ugly hull sat under clean Aerit light, full of beef, wine, illegal parts, and exactly the wrong amount of destiny.

On The Rim, Vigor Hallman served dinner to Aphex soldiers and pretended to be only a farmer.

Somewhere in Aphex records, a forty-two-year-old materials specialist continued a normal day under a false sky, unaware that Black Hole B-7 had found a pattern in their bloodwork.

And far away, or perhaps not far at all, a temporary body stood in a locked room for less than two seconds and left behind particles that folded into nothing.

This time, when the residue vanished, someone was watching the watchers.

Chapter 17: Audit Cover

The brown dwarf did not look like much. That was the point.

It hung ahead of the Fastulv as a swollen ember in infrared, too cold to be a star, too massive to be ignored. Old mining claims, research platforms, and corporate infrastructure wrapped it. They had learned to look boring for tax reasons. Aphex Defence called the facility `Dependency L-Prime 41`. Lars called it a filing cabinet with engines.

The visible approach was Fastulv's job.

Havets Sista Sång waited elsewhere, if elsewhere meant anything inside a fold path that touched ordinary space only when it felt like being polite. Lars could not see the old ship on any display. He knew it was there because command access sat in the back of his mind like a cold hand on a rail.

Grim sat beside him in the copilot's chair, pretending to run ordinary navigation. In reality he was linked through Fastulv, Havets, and a thin relay back to Arkology Zero. His metal body had chosen to squeak one knee joint for public audio pickup, which Lars found both thoughtful and insulting.

"You are overdoing the defective robot act," Lars said.

"I am performing mediocrity."

"That is my line."

"You taught me."

On the forward display, the Aphex facility expanded into view: three habitation cylinders, two research spines, a shielded materials ring, a small defense dock, and a corporate chapel module added by some executive committee that believed workers became more ethical when architecture looked expensive.

Valdrun's voice came through the hidden channel from Havets.

"Remember the plan."

"I adore when people say that to me as if it has ever worked."

"Lars."

"Fine. Audit cover. No ancient savior routine. No public Aerit hints. Consent before unlock. Local intervention only. Do not start a war unless the war starts first and signs the forms."

Grim turned his head. "That last clause was not in the plan."

"It was implied."

Aphex traffic control hailed them with a nervous woman whose voice had the brittle calm of someone reading from emergency procedure while senior management watched.

"Fastulv, this is Dependency L-Prime 41 Control. Confirm identity and audit authorization."

Lars opened the board-level packet Gabriel Arsenault had sent three hours earlier. It contained enough signatures, seals, emergency clauses, legal threats, and French phrasing to make a lawyer weep into silk.

"L-Prime Control, this is Captain Lars Odin, Aphex Defence board director, strategic integrity audit authority under post-attack emergency powers, arriving with one freighter, one robot, and a bad attitude."

The channel remained silent for half a second too long.

"Confirmed, Director Odin. Please hold for security escort."

"Holding sounds inefficient."

"Director?"

"Never mind. Holding."

Grim muted the channel. "You enjoy making clerks afraid."

"No. I enjoy making systems reveal stress points."

"That is what frightening clerks means when said by a rich person."

"Noted."

A military cutter detached from the defense dock and moved toward them. Its transponder identified it as Aphex Defence, Eleventh Brigade.

Lars smiled.

"Oh good."

Major Elliot Fabron appeared on the comm a moment later, helmet off, uniform immaculate, expression already tired.

"Monsieur Odin."

"Major Fabron. You look well for a man whose report used 'recklessly effective' seven times."

Fabron's left eye twitched.

"I stand by the wording."

"I framed it."

"I am your assigned security liaison for the duration of this audit."

"Did you volunteer?"

"No."

"Then someone likes me."

"Or dislikes me."

Fabron's cutter escorted them toward Dock Two. Lars let him. The facility's passive scans washed over Fastulv: hull, cargo, declared weapons, undeclared weapons pretending to be declared maintenance systems, and at least six things that should have triggered alarms but did not because Grim had written them lullabies.

Inside his hidden channel, Valdrun said, "Fabron is observing your scan behavior."

"He always observes."

"He is better than last time."

"Last time he was trying to rescue me from people I had already killed."

"That can motivate self-improvement."

The docking clamps engaged.

Lars stood, adjusted his black coat, checked that the Ragnarrök was visibly powered down but emotionally present, and looked at Grim.

"Simple service automaton."

Grim's servos whined. "With no unusual interests except food."

"And audit support."

"And audit support."

The airlock opened.

Fabron waited on the other side with eight armored Aphex soldiers, two legal observers, one medical compliance officer, and a station administrator who looked as if she had slept in a chair designed by accountants.

Fabron glanced at the Ragnarrök.

"You brought that."

"It gets anxious alone."

"This is a research facility."

"Then it will learn something."

The administrator stepped forward. "Director Odin, I am Administrator Celia Marchand. We have prepared the audit suite, personnel records, laboratory access, and secure conference rooms."

"Good. I want three things first. Full list of personnel with unusual medical exceptions over the last ninety years. Materials fatigue labs. And whatever passes for steak on this facility."

Marchand blinked. "Steak?"

Fabron closed his eyes.

Grim leaned toward a wall sensor and squeaked.

"The audit," Lars said, "will be severe."

Marchand clearly could not decide which part of that sentence was a joke.

Excellent.

They led him through the facility's main spine. L-Prime 41 smelled of filtered metal, coffee concentrate, anxious humans, and cold research money. Aphex branding appeared everywhere, but less aggressively than on public stations. This place did not sell confidence. It manufactured things that made confidence unnecessary.

Lars saw the stress points immediately.

One power loop too centralized.

Two emergency corridors rerouted for executive security.

Medical archive latency inconsistent with declared privacy policy.

Materials ring shielded well enough against radiation, poorly enough against someone already inside.

And under it all, a pattern in small things: workers had added manual overrides where corporate design preferred locks, food gardens in ventilation dead spaces, redundant local tool caches, unofficial mutual-aid boards, and handwritten maintenance tags with tiny spiral marks in the corners.

Återviens habits.

Not named.

Not official.

Alive.

Lars did not look at Fabron.

Fabron noticed anyway.

The audit suite overlooked the materials ring through pressure glass. A dozen researchers moved below among test rigs, vacuum chambers, stress tables, and fatigue simulators. One of them stood beside a cracked composite beam, arguing with three engineers and a wall display.

Forty-two on record.

Five hundred to seven hundred in probability.

She had dark copper skin, close-cropped silver hair, and the stillness of someone who did not waste movement. Her ID tag read:

DR. MIRA SOVAN

MATERIALS FATIGUE SPECIALIST

She tapped the cracked beam once and said something that made all three engineers stop arguing.

Lars could not hear through the glass.

He did not need to.

He knew the shape of the gesture.

Someone solving the problem before everyone else understood the question.

Grim's private voice entered his head.

Candidate probability just rose.

Lars watched Dr. Mira Sovan turn away from the beam.

For the briefest moment she looked up, through the pressure glass, directly at him.

No recognition.

But her hand went to the back of her neck.

Fabron said quietly, "Is that who you came for?"

Lars did not turn.

"Major, that is a very dangerous question."

"Then I will ask a safer one."

"Good."

"Should I be protecting her from you?"

Lars finally looked at him.

Fabron's face was stiff, formal, irritatingly French.

But his eyes were not.

Lars said, "Possibly."

Chapter 18: Materials Fatigue

Dr. Mira Sovan trusted cracks more than people.

Cracks did not lie. They branched under stress, wandered along grain boundaries, slowed at good welds, accelerated through vanity engineering, and told patient stories about force, heat, time, and neglect. People told stories too, but usually to avoid invoices.

The composite beam on the test table had failed seventeen hours before its certified fatigue limit.

That was not supposed to happen.

Three engineers had spent the morning blaming the supplier, the simulator, the radiation profile, the junior technician who calibrated the loading cradle, and once, with impressive cowardice, the cleaning crew.

Mira had listened for six minutes, which was generous.

Then she tapped the crack with one gloved finger.

"The beam is innocent."

Silence.

Tomar, the youngest engineer, blinked. "The beam is what?"

"Innocent." She enlarged the crack path on the wall display. "It did what it was told to do."

"It failed early."

"Because the test lied."

Senior Engineer Bellac frowned. "The simulator logs are clean."

"Clean logs are not the same as true logs."

She rotated the model. Heat maps unfolded across the beam. The visible crack became a red vein through layered structure. She added acoustic data, then vibration history, then a maintenance timestamp from a loading arm that had not been part of the official test packet.

"There," she said.

No one saw it.

Of course they did not.

Mira sighed. "The loading arm compensated for a micro-stutter in its left actuator. The cradle software smoothed the compensation. The simulator believed the load curve was continuous. It was not. Every seventy-third cycle, the beam received a lateral impulse too small to trigger alarms and too regular to be noise."

Bellac stared.

Tomar whispered, "That's not in the logs."

"Yes," Mira said. "That is why we are having this conversation."

She did not mention that she had known the answer before opening the acoustic layer.

That was a private problem.

Private problems belonged in locked drawers, encrypted notes, and very long walks through maintenance corridors where nobody asked why she had solved a six-week failure review between coffee and lunch.

Her neck ached.

It had been doing that more often.

Not like injury. Like an old warning system built by someone who disliked explanations.

"Replace the actuator," she said. "Disable smoothing during certification tests. Notify the supplier that their cradle firmware is hiding impulse artifacts. And apologize to the cleaning crew."

Bellac looked wounded. "I did not officially blame them."

"Then unofficially apologize."

Tomar grinned.

Mira liked Tomar. He was young, nervous, and still believed competence should improve institutions. That kind of innocence either died or became stubborn enough to matter.

Her console chimed.

Strategic integrity audit.

Board-level.

Emergency authority.

Director Lars Odin.

Mira stared at the name.

She knew it, of course. Everyone at Aphex did. The influencer-turned-billionaire-turned-weapons-board-menace who had apparently solved Professor Fayolle's impossible Ragnarrök problem over coffee and then used tactical nuclear weapons near The Rim because subtlety had been out of stock.

Everyone in French weapons circles also knew the Ragnarrök was not simply a gun. It was a national embarrassment, national miracle, and budgetary wound with a grip. Aphex officers pretended to discuss it soberly, then lowered their voices like priests near expensive sin.

She had expected him to look like an expensive idiot.

When she glanced up through the pressure glass, he stood in the audit suite above the ring wearing a black coat, bad posture, and the expression of a man pretending not to understand exactly how every machine in the room could fail.

For a moment their eyes met.

Something moved at the edge of memory.

Not a memory.

A pressure.

Her hand rose to the back of her neck before she could stop it.

The ache faded.

Strange.

"Doctor?" Tomar asked.

"Send the actuator report," she said.

"To audit?"

"To everyone before audit asks. It looks better if we discover our own mistakes before the rich man in sunglasses discovers them for us."

"He is not wearing sunglasses."

Mira looked up again.

Lars Odin was now speaking to Major Fabron, who stood beside him with the rigid suffering of a man assigned to escort a natural disaster. The Ragnarrök rode Lars's back like a classified insult. Fabron's eyes flicked to it once with something that was almost pride, then immediately became French disapproval, which Mira suspected was the same emotion wearing a better coat. A robot stood behind them, servos whining with theatrical incompetence.

The robot turned its head toward her.

Too precisely.

Mira narrowed her eyes.

"Interesting."

The robot immediately bumped one shoulder against a wall panel and produced a sad little squeak.

Too late.

Mira smiled despite herself.

The audit reached her lab nine minutes later.

Administrator Marchand entered first, then two legal observers, then Major Fabron, then Lars Odin and his robot. Marchand was not the exhausted committee ghost Mira had expected. She was tall, dark-eyed, and beautifully put together in the expensive, dangerous way of a woman who could make a budget hearing feel underdressed. The legal observers looked frightened of paperwork. Fabron looked frightened of Lars. Lars looked disappointed by the room's coffee machine.

"Dr. Sovan," Marchand said, voice warm enough to be human and precise enough to be dangerous, "Director Odin has requested access to materials fatigue protocols and personnel exception records."

"Those are different audits."

Lars looked at the cracked beam. "Everything is the same audit if the auditors are annoying enough."

Mira studied him.

Up close, he did not look like his public feeds. The face was the same, the hair was the same, the irreverent mouth unfortunately the same, but the eyes were wrong. Too old? No. That was absurd. He looked thirtyish. Maybe a rough thirtyish. A wealthy thirtyish who slept badly and bought coats as camouflage.

Still, something in his eyes had weather.

"You solved the beam," he said.

"Yes."

"Actuator micro-stutter."

The room went quiet.

Mira folded her arms. "You read the report?"

"No."

"Then how did you know?"

Lars glanced at the beam. "The crack path is sulking."

Tomar made a small sound that might have been religious.

Mira did not smile.

"Cracks do not sulk."

"That one does."

"Cracks propagate."

"And occasionally complain."

The robot leaned toward Fabron and whispered too loudly, "This is how specialists flirt."

Fabron's jaw tightened.

Lars pointed backward. "Simple service automaton. Very defective."

Fabron's eyes dropped, despite himself, to the Ragnarrök's inert spine housing. "The automaton is defective. The weapon is merely... inelegantly carried."

"That sounded almost like affection," Lars said.

"For French engineering, not for you."

"Clearly," Mira said.

The robot's optical lenses brightened for a fraction of a second.

Not simple.

Not defective.

Interesting.

Lars walked around the table without touching anything. He kept his hands visible, which Mira appreciated because board directors often believed proximity was permission. He looked at the beam, the cradle, the actuator housing, the acoustic logs still open on the wall.

"Good catch," he said.

"I know."

Marchand cleared her throat. "Director, perhaps the personnel records—"

"In a minute." Lars looked back at Mira. "How long have you worked here?"

"Eight years."

"Before that?"

"NUKEA arbitration habitat. Reactor casing failure review."

"Before that?"

"University of New Stockholm. Materials aging."

"Before that?"

Mira's neck tightened again, but less than usual. "Several places."

"You move when systems get too stupid."

That hit.

She kept her face still. "I move when contracts end."

"Of course."

Fabron watched them both.

Too carefully.

Mira shifted her attention to him. "Major, is this audit a technical review or an interrogation?"

Fabron answered before Lars could. "Today it is technical. If it becomes an interrogation, I will say so."

That was unexpectedly decent.

Lars seemed pleased.

Mira disliked that she noticed.

Her console chimed again. This time the alert was private: a sealed medical compliance request, board authority, ninety-year personnel anomaly cross-check.

Her stomach went cold.

"No," she said.

Marchand looked at her. "Doctor?"

Mira opened the request. It was legal. Worse, it was elegant. It requested not medical content but exception metadata: unusual aging exemptions, radiation exposure survivals, regenerative anomalies, missing childhood records, classified medical deferrals.

Not enough to expose her.

Enough to point.

Mira looked at Lars.

He was not smiling now.

"Why are you here?" she asked.

The lab noise seemed to recede. Tomar stopped pretending to work. Bellac looked at the floor. Fabron's hand rested near his sidearm without touching it. The robot had gone completely still.

Lars said, "Because someone worse may already be looking."

Mira felt the sentence move through the room.

Not threat.

Warning.

She had spent her life avoiding mysteries about herself. She had changed jobs when questions accumulated. She had learned which doctors to avoid, which records to corrupt, which jokes to make when people said she looked younger than her file implied. She had told herself everyone had gaps.

Everyone did not have gaps shaped like locked doors.

"Director Odin," she said carefully, "if you have an accusation, make it."

"I don't."

"If you have a question, ask it."

Lars looked at her for a long moment.

Then, softly enough that only she, Fabron, and the robot could hear, he said:

"Coherence?"

The word meant nothing.

It meant everything.

Mira's hand closed around the edge of the table hard enough that the composite creaked.

For one impossible second, she smelled cold stone, reactor oil, and bread in a corridor she had never walked.

Then it was gone.

She stepped back.

"Get out."

Tomar flinched.

Marchand began, "Dr. Sovan—"

"Out," Mira said again, and every engineer in the lab found a reason to obey.

Lars did not move.

Neither did Fabron.

Neither did the robot.

Mira looked at the cracked beam because cracks were honest and people were not.

"I don't know what that word is," she said.

Lars nodded.

"I believe you."

"Then why did you say it?"

"Because if you had answered, we would be having a different conversation."

Mira laughed once. It sounded sharp and unlike her.

"And what conversation are we having now?"

The facility lights flickered.

Not much.

Enough.

Grim turned toward the ceiling.

Fabron's comm chirped with three alerts at once.

Lars looked toward the materials ring door.

"The one where someone else stops waiting."

Chapter 19: Fabron's Orders

Major Elliot Fabron hated coincidences because commanders used them to hide incompetence.

The facility lights flickered once.

His comm opened three alerts.

Power regulation variance in Materials Ring B.

External telemetry dropout across Dock Two.

Medical archive access conflict, source masked.

Three unrelated failures inside four seconds.

Not coincidence.

"Lock the ring," Fabron said.

Administrator Marchand, who had returned halfway through the evacuation of the lab and now looked ready to resign into a ventilation duct, touched her wrist console. "Security shutters are responding."

They were not.

Fabron watched the pressure glass darken halfway, stop, shudder, then retreat.

"Manual."

"Manual teams are two decks away."

"Then they should run."

Lars Odin had gone very still.

That concerned Fabron more than if the man had drawn the absurd weapon on his back and declared war on architecture. Lars's jokes were noise. His stillness was signal.

The robot was also still.

Simple service automata did not become still like predators.

Dr. Mira Sovan stood beside the cracked beam with one hand on the table. She looked frightened, yes, but not like a civilian. Like someone whose body remembered drills her mind had never attended.

Fabron added that to the growing list of things he disliked.

"Director," he said, "you will come with me to secure conference."

"No."

"That was not an invitation."

"I know. It was a bad order."

Fabron stepped closer. "My orders are to keep you alive."

"From whom?"

"Everyone, apparently."

"Then start with her."

Lars nodded toward Sovan.

Fabron followed the gesture. "Doctor Sovan is Aphex personnel."

"So was the blood-harvester dart."

Fabron did not react outwardly. He had read the sealed report. Not all of it; Arsenault had redacted enough to make the document legally smug. But enough. The attack near The Rim. Black Hole B-7. Biological sampling. Lars Odin and his impossible circle taking a weapon alive.

"Do you have evidence she is targeted?"

"Not evidence. Pattern."

"Patterns do not authorize action."

"They authorize attention."

Fabron hated that because it was true.

His comm opened a fourth alert.

Security feed loop on Habitation Cylinder Three.

He opened command frequency. "Fabron to Security Actual. Confirm teams at Ring B manual shutters."

Static.

Then a voice. "Confirm, Major. Teams en route."

Too smooth.

Fabron said, "Authentication phrase."

A pause.

"Say again?"

"Authentication phrase."

"Major, we have an emergency—"

Fabron cut the channel. "Compromised."

Marchand went pale.

Lars looked almost pleased. "You are less decorative than you pretend."

"I have never pretended to be decorative."

"The uniform does a lot of work."

"Enough."

Fabron changed to local squad channel. "Team Three, silent protocol. Two hostiles probable inside response net. Protect Doctor Sovan. Treat Director Odin as uncontrolled friendly asset."

Lars raised a finger. "Uncontrolled?"

"Would you prefer unstable?"

"Friendly asset is also optimistic."

The robot made a sound like a cough, which no robot needed to make.

Fabron ignored it.

He had larger problems. One of them was the second order folded inside his first.

The first order came from Aphex Defence: protect Lars Odin, maintain board authority, secure evidence, prevent exposure of classified Aphex vulnerabilities.

The second had arrived twelve minutes before Fastulv docked, on a channel Fabron had not used since childhood.

It contained no Aphex seal.

No sender name.

Only seven lines of old liturgical shorthand that would have looked like religious nonsense to anyone outside the Order of the Clear Hand.

Keep the hinge small.

Do not centralize rescue.

Protect the sleeping engineer.

Refuse the throne.

If the black wolf comes, count his teeth.

If the old word is spoken, listen.

Repair before worship.

Fabron had read it three times, hated the poetry, verified the encryption chain, and then stared at the fifth line until his coffee went cold.

The black wolf.

Fastulv.

Lars Odin's ridiculous freighter.

His grandmother would have said the old instructions did not predict; they preserved shapes. Patterns passed down through ritual because institutions burned records faster than families forgot prayers.

Fabron was a modern officer.

He believed in chain of command, logistics, training, ballistics, and audited reactor maintenance.

He also carried a small steel token under his uniform, stamped with three interlocking circles and a crack deliberately left unrepaired.

Repair before worship.

His family had said it at meals.

His training brothers had said it over broken pumps, jammed doors, failed exams, and dead batteries. It meant do the work. It meant do not confuse reverence with maintenance. It meant systems survived because ordinary people made them survive.

Now Lars Odin had spoken a word in an unknown language and Dr. Sovan had nearly crushed a composite table with one hand.

Fabron did not enjoy history becoming operational.

"Major," Marchand said, "Dock Two has lost telemetry."

"Of course it has."

Lars turned toward Grim. "Fastulv?"

"Apparently parked, silent, and deeply offended," the robot said, forgetting to squeak.

Marchand stared.

Grim squeaked immediately.

"Defective," Lars said.

"I hate all of you," Fabron said.

That restored him slightly.

The lab door opened.

Two Aphex soldiers entered. Fabron recognized their armor markings but not their gait. Too balanced. Too quiet. Their rifles were held at the correct angle, but their fingers were wrong on the grip, as if their bodies had downloaded training without earning habit.

"Stop," Fabron ordered.

They did not.

Lars said, "Those are not yours."

"I noticed."

Fabron fired first.

Not at the soldiers.

At the floor between them.

The round burst into a white ceramic foam barrier, expanding waist-high across the lab entrance. The two false soldiers reacted with inhuman speed, vaulting the barrier as it rose.

Mira moved.

She grabbed the cracked composite beam, twisted, and drove one broken end into the lead hostile's helmet joint. The motion was ugly, efficient, and far stronger than Aphex medical records allowed.

The hostile struck her across the shoulder.

She hit the test table, rolled, and came up furious.

Lars drew the Ragnarrök halfway.

Fabron snapped, "No heavy weapon discharge inside a pressure ring!"

"You take the fun out of everything."

"Correct."

The robot crossed the room in a blur, defective act abandoned, and hit the second hostile with enough force to throw it into a vibration rig. The rig screamed. Metal folded. The hostile did not scream. Its armor split at the neck and released a dark thread of particulate matter that crawled upward instead of falling.

Black Hole.

Or something wearing its habits.

Fabron's squad finally arrived in the corridor. Real this time; he knew by the annoyed breathing of Sergeant Mbeki before she even spoke.

"Major?"

"Containment foam, doorline, now. Medical ring lockdown. Nobody enters on remote orders."

"Yes, sir."

The first hostile rose despite the beam lodged near its neck. Mira stared at it, breathing hard.

Her eyes had changed.

Not color. Focus.

Lars saw it too.

"Mira," he said.

She flinched at the name, not because it was familiar, but because it was spoken as if he had decided she mattered.

"Do not tell me what I am," she said.

"I wasn't going to."

"Good."

"Duck."

She ducked.

Lars fired the Ragnarrök at minimum yield.

Minimum was still offensive.

The beam struck the hostile's upper torso and converted armor, cloned tissue, and crawling particulate into an expanding cloud of inert glitter that slammed against the containment foam and rained down like expensive dust.

Fabron stared.

"Minimum yield," Lars said.

"I am writing you up."

"Use adjectives."

The second hostile twitched under the vibration rig. Grim pinned it with one metal foot.

"Alive enough for interrogation," Grim said.

Fabron looked at the robot.

"You are not a service automaton."

Grim turned one lens toward Lars. "My cover is compromised."

"Your cover was a squeaky knee and a dream."

The facility alarms finally understood they had been late and began screaming.

Fabron opened a secure channel to Arsenault.

No response.

He opened Aphex command.

Delayed.

He opened the private channel from the Order.

Silent.

That, more than anything, frightened him.

Fabron looked at Lars, at Mira, at the black particulate dissolving into nothing, at his soldiers forming a human wall because he had trained them well enough for at least one thing in this cursed day to work.

"Doctor Sovan," he said, "you are under my protection."

Mira gave him a look sharp enough to cut hull plating.

"I did not ask for protection."

"No. You are receiving it anyway until you are calm enough to refuse intelligently."

Lars smiled.

Fabron pointed at him. "Do not approve of me."

"Too late."

"Director Odin, you will explain what this is."

Lars looked down at the glittering remains.

"No," he said.

Fabron's hand tightened around his sidearm.

Lars met his eyes.

"Not until she says yes."

Mira stood very still.

Fabron understood, then. Not the science. Not the history. Not the impossible age hiding inside a materials specialist's bloodwork.

But the line.

Consent before rescue.

Repair before worship.

He lowered his weapon.

"Then ask properly."

Chapter 20: The First Ask

The secure conference room had been built to survive lawsuits, hostage events, vacuum breach, and senior executives explaining budget decisions.

It was not where Valdrun would have put a retrieval operation.

That was why they did not use it as one.

Dr. Mira Sovan occupied the far end of the table with one shoulder strapped, one hand wrapped, and the expression of a woman deciding which weapon in the room would be least annoying to use. Major Fabron stood near the door with two soldiers outside, one inside, and enough suspicion to power emergency lighting. Grim stood beside the wall display like a polished metal crime. Lars leaned against the table because sitting felt too much like a board meeting, and he had survived enough horrors without adding proper posture.

That was the visible scene.

The real one had already divided itself into lanes.

Havets Sista Sång had folded its attention around the med bay where the captured Black Hole infiltrator lay in a containment coffin, alive enough to be a problem. Mira was in the conference room, injured, angry, and very much not a prisoner. Ödesvaka watched Arkology-side consent gates through a link so narrow it could barely be called a link. Grim ran the boundary monitor and sanitized every data layer before it touched the room. Fabron held Aphex local command, med security, legal cover, and the right to say no if Lars became an ancient disaster in a black coat.

Valdrun was only a voice in Lars's hidden channel.

That was deliberate.

An ancient half-machine guardian appearing in person would turn a careful knock into a hammer.

"Quiet Knock," Valdrun said.

Lars heard more in the old man's two words than anyone else in the room could. Not a title. A protocol. A memory of Alpha Fourteen engineers who had known their own mercy might become a prison.

Finding locked Aerits had never been the impossible part.

They still carried the nanobot line. Even buried under cover lives and hypnosis, those machines remained faintly coupled to the larger Aerit network and, beyond it, the older mindfield Lyra had only partly understood. Locked minds did not broadcast names, memories, or addresses. But the nanobots left pressure: background coherence, repair signatures, route drift, impossible survivals, small alignments that only made sense across decades.

The hard part was not finding the sleepers.

The hard part was knocking without making the lock defend itself.

This was not what had happened to Lars.

Lars had reached Station Zero half-broken by emergency repair, old command access, Valdrun's presence, Ödesvaka's authority, and medical hardware built for the lock itself. His dehypnotization had been surgery inside the original hospital, performed because the lock and his body were already fighting hard enough to kill him.

Mira had none of that here.

No Station Zero chamber. No guardian in the room. No helmet. No safe surgical map of her lock. No right to open anything just because danger had arrived.

Quiet Knock was not dehypnotization.

It was how not to do dehypnotization by accident.

"Layer one," Grim said in the public channel.

The wall display lit, but it did not show Mira's old face, sealed files, impossible ages, or any word that could hit the lock like a blade. It showed a narrow operational panel.

NETWORK SIGNAL: CANDIDATE LOCATED

QUIET KNOCK: CURRENT LIFE ONLY

CONSENT GATE: PERSON CONTROLS NEXT LAYER

Mira read it once.

"Person," she said, as if testing whether they meant it.

"Person," Lars said. "Grim was raised in tanks and became rude through paperwork."

"Correction accepted under protest," Grim said.

Mira did not smile. Her attention stayed on the display the way a good engineer watched a load test: not trusting the frame, not trusting the numbers, not trusting the men standing too close to it.

"Start," she said.

Lars wanted to answer.

Valdrun spoke first, privately. "No history. No identity. Ask for present work."

That hurt more than it should have. Lars wanted, stupidly, to tell her enough to make the shape of danger fair. He wanted to say Aerit, old war, locks, Lyra, blood harvest, all the words that had nearly killed him before they saved him.

He did not.

Competence, he was learning, sometimes meant leaving the dramatic sentence in its cage.

"You were attacked because someone else noticed enough of your signal to come hunting," Lars said. "We are not asking your blood to explain itself. We are asking whether you will help solve a current engineering problem under boundaries you set."

Mira's eyes moved from him to Grim.

"Clarify the signal without naming it."

Grim highlighted a redacted signal map. "Station Zero and the quiet-node mesh can hear active nanobot carriers as weak coherence signatures. Hidden arkology fragments, guardian stations, low-noise observatories, dormant relay caches, and old ships all listen from different angles. Your signal is one of seventeen strong candidates across ninety years. It identifies a probable carrier. It does not reveal identity, memory, consent, or ownership."

"What does it hear?"

"Not words," Grim said. "Not thoughts. Nanobot timing drift. Repair-cycle harmonics. Biological error correction too clean for ordinary medicine. Route choices bending toward low-noise systems. Professional problem-solving with the old shape. Enough to locate probability. Not enough to open a person."

Mira's fingers flattened on the table.

"That is surveillance."

"Yes," Lars said.

The room tightened around the honesty.

He let it.

"It is also the reason we found you before the people who tried to harvest you got a second attempt. Both things are true. One does not clean the other."

Fabron's face changed by one millimeter: approval filed where no one could use it against him.

Mira looked at the next column.

"Quiet Knock."

"The network can find you," Lars said. "That does not give us the right to touch what it found. The knock has to fit the life you know, or it is just another violation wearing clever shoes."

Grim added three current items:

MATERIALS RING SENSOR ACCESS

HOSTILE HULL FATIGUE CLASSIFICATION

NO BIOLOGICAL INFERENCE / NO IDENTITY LANGUAGE

"No old names," Grim said. "No direct contradiction of cover memory. No identity prompts. No memory work. If distress crosses your boundary, the system stops."

"Whose system?"

"Mine," Grim said. "Fabron's witness record. A sealed offsite veto you do not have to name. Your local vault if you approve."

"And him?" Mira nodded toward Lars.

"He gets overruled," Fabron said.

Lars pointed at him. "He has been waiting all day to say that."

"Longer."

Mira's mouth almost moved.

Not a smile.

A structural test passing under load.

Then the lights flickered.

This time the emergency system did not recover cleanly. A low tone pulsed through the wall. Fabron's comm lit with fresh alerts, three at once.

"External approach," he said. "Three signatures. No transponders."

Grim's head tilted. "Fast. Badly concealed. Aggressively biological."

"Black Hole?" Lars asked.

"Or someone imitating them with poor taste."

The operation shifted without anyone needing to declare it dramatic.

"Lane two active," Valdrun said in Lars's ear. "Do not pull her. Let the work invite her."

Lars pushed off the table.

"Doctor Sovan. Current problem. Three ships are approaching through the brown dwarf's particle wake. We need hull behavior classification from local fatigue sensors. You may refuse. You may limit scope. We will not ask what you are. We will not ask what you remember."

Mira's gaze went to the wall, then to the pressure glass, then to the vibration monitor mounted above the materials ring. Her face changed, not into revelation, not into destiny, but into work.

Work was safer than wonder.

"Scope," she said.

Grim answered before Lars could. "Current hull stress, drive compensation, sampling geometry, station structural risk. Excluded: blood inference, personal identity inference, memory correlation, old-language triggers."

"Stop condition?"

"You say stop," Fabron said. "Or the monitor flags distress. Or I judge the room unsafe."

"You?"

"Yes."

"That is irritatingly useful."

"I cultivate it."

Mira looked at Lars. "If I say no, do those ships still arrive?"

"Yes."

"If I say yes, do I become your tool?"

There it was.

Not fear alone. Not accusation alone. A person feeling the edges of a machine built around her without permission and demanding to know whether the next machine would be different.

Lars made himself answer without charm.

"No. You become a specialist choosing a bounded task under hostile conditions. Afterward you can throw us out, ask one question, or never speak to me again."

"That last option has appeal."

Mira breathed once through her nose. Her shoulder hurt; Lars could see it in the controlled angle of her posture. Her hand was wrapped from the fight. Her life had been invaded by false soldiers, impossible metadata, polite military protection, and a rude man refusing to say the dangerous word she could feel waiting behind the room.

She still reached for the console.

"Current evidence only," she said. "Hull behavior. Station risk. No blood. No identity. No old language. No clever nudges."

Grim's lenses brightened. "Consent boundary recorded."

"Do not sound pleased."

"Pleased state suppressed."

"Badly."

Fabron opened a local command bridge to the materials ring and cut Aphex central out of the loop with the elegance of a man committing treason through proper procedure.
