"Doctor Sovan has provisional technical authority over structural interpretation," he said. "Adjacent to my command."

"Adjacent," Mira said.

"Under would be inaccurate."

"Better."

Lars moved toward the door.

"Grim, clean local data only. Fabron, med bay hard seal and no remote orders. Havets, keep being creepy and useful. Valdrun, tell me if I start acting like a throne-shaped idiot."

"I have been doing so for centuries," Valdrun said.

"I meant more urgently."

"No."

Mira's fingers paused over the controls.

"Director Odin."

He stopped.

"Earlier. The word you almost used."

He knew which one.

Coherence.

The old greeting sat behind his teeth like a lit match.

"I won't say it unless you ask."

She nodded once.

Not trust.

A temporary load rating.

"Good."

Outside the conference room, the station shifted into emergency posture. Doors sealed. Soldiers moved. Remote command began issuing instructions that local hands were already learning to distrust.

Somewhere beyond the hull, three silent ships entered the brown dwarf's dim and dirty light.

Inside the room, Mira Sovan began reading their bones through stress.

It was not an awakening.

Not yet.

It was a bounded task.

It was the first ask.

Chapter 21: Containment Drill

The three incoming ships arrived like bad decisions wearing stealth plating.

They came out of the brown dwarf's dirty light without a clean drive plume. They slid between particle gusts and sensor blind spots. L-Prime 41 was a mining dependency, not a fortress. Its outer rings held materials labs, rotating crew berths, refinery blisters, and repair yards layered around whatever Aphex had underfunded last decade. Through the filters, the whole station smelled of hot metal, old coolant, and too many people trying not to breathe loudly.

Mira saw the ships first as fatigue signatures.

Their hulls flexed against the wake in three incompatible rhythms: built, grown, and stolen. Someone had forced them into one fleet. That someone believed maintenance was weakness. The stress display did not show enemies. It showed bad joins and dishonest composite. Living tissue had been welded to factory frame. A third hull looked normal only if you trusted surfaces more than load.

"Black Hole," Lars said. "That hull is lying with both hands."

"Director, hull stress is not a legal identification method," Fabron snapped over the tactical channel.

"She reads bones. I insult criminals."

Mira did not look away from the console. The materials lab had become a local sensor station, not a command center. Her instruments were better at telling structural truth than Aphex's command software. If she was Aerit, she was still locked: no old Aerit channels, no Arkology feed, no hidden history except the few current facts she had consented to hold. Two soldiers had wedged a table against the door. Someone's forgotten tea floated in a sealed bulb beside a rack of impact coupons. The emergency lights painted the room red every four seconds.

Grim's boundary monitor stayed green: current hull behavior only, no blood, no identity, no memory language.

"Still inside scope," Mira said, before anyone could ask.

"Logged," Grim replied. "I am not enjoying restraint. I am performing it."

Fabron's voice came tight through the channel. "All local detachments, containment posture. Manual confirmation only. If central sends contradictory instructions, you will ask me before obeying the distant idiot."

"Major," an Aphex lieutenant said, scandalized and relieved at once, "that phrasing is not regulation."

"Then remember the meaning and forget the phrasing."

Fastulv detached from Dock Two with every running light bright, every transponder loud, and its visible weapons arranged like an elderly freighter trying very hard to look suicidal. Lars wanted the hostile ships to see the wolf. He wanted them to believe all the old violence was outside, rusty and theatrical, away from the station's soft blood.

Havets Sista Sång stayed absent from Aphex sensors, a hidden pressure behind the station's skin.

The old ship did not announce itself. It slid its attention through maintenance relays, hand overrides, chapel kitchen circuits, station guild tags, and old local caches Fabron's Clear Hand people had kept as ritual without knowing why. On one deck, a cook saw a freezer light blink twice and closed the manual fire doors before the remote panel woke. In a refinery crawlspace, a pipefitter felt the vibration pattern change. He spun a brass wheel his apprentice had been told never to touch unless the air tasted like pennies.

Doors stopped obeying remote orders.

Shutters accepted hand-cranked authority.

Emergency lights pulsed in a rhythm workers understood before anyone explained it: isolate, verify, repair.

"Your station is committing disobedience in an organized manner," Grim said.

Fabron exhaled. "Good."

The first hostile launched a sampling drone toward the materials ring. Not a missile. A needle with engines. It skipped around point-defense prediction, cold until the last instant, then opened a throat lined with soft black vanes.

Mira's fingers tightened on the edge of the console. "Sampling throat opening along the warmest pressure boundary. It wants atmosphere first, blood second. Point defense is still waiting for some remote idiot to bless local physics."

"Remote safety authority has failed again," Grim said. "I am logging it under civilization, recurring defects."

"Fastulv," Lars said.

The freighter rolled, dumped two empty cargo pods, and heated them just enough to look like panicked evacuation mistakes. The drone chose the warmer target. Fastulv's belly turret cut it into vapor, and the shock flashed white across the lab windows before the shutters came down.

People cheered in three rooms. Fabron killed that with one word.

"Focus."

The second hostile learned faster. It kept its distance and extended a longer filament probe, thin enough to hide in the sensor noise, aiming for the materials ring's intake baffles. If it reached the air system, it could sample every crew member in the lab deck before anyone bled.

Mira leaned closer to her board.

"I can make the ring look injured," she said. "Cracked braces, phantom torsion, heat gradients wrong enough to invite a greedy system."

"Risk to station?" Fabron asked.

"If I do it badly, we lose a brace and three labs. If I do it well, it exposes a sampling throat."

"Do it well," Lars said.

Mira gave him a look sharp enough to score alloy.

"By permission," he corrected. "Your call."

Her jaw worked once. "My call. Grim, give me the clean sensor lanes and stop decorating them."

"My labels are informative."

"Your labels are noise."

"Noise removed. Emotion noted and categorized as justified irritation."

Mira fed the second hostile a false song of cracked braces, phantom torsion, and impossible heat gradients. It was not ancient instinct. It was a dirty certification trick turned inside out by a woman who had spent decades catching lies in beams, cradles, and reactor casings. It took three long minutes. The station groaned for real in places that did not know they were lying. A child cried over an open channel until someone hushed the line. Lars stood in the docking throat to Fastulv, helmet sealed, Ragnarrök inert at his thigh, and waited for the enemy to believe in an injury.

It opened a sampling throat wider.

Havets touched the probe.

No beam appeared. No miracle filled the glass. The probe's control loops simply forgot how to agree with each other. The throat clenched, opened, clenched again, and spat its own steering filament into the brown dwarf wake. The hostile ship slewed sideways. Its flank was exposed. Fastulv fired a shot that cut grown tissue away from stolen frame. The frame spun away trailing atmosphere, coolant, and red-black droplets that boiled into nothing.

The third hostile ran.

Fabron ordered pursuit. Lars countermanded it before Fastulv could burn.

"It will report our response posture," Fabron said. "That is an operational exposure."

"Yes. It will report that Fastulv is the loud problem, Aphex can still hurt people when headquarters is stupid, and Dr. Sovan remains protected by consent instead of stolen open."

On the lab screen, the third ship dwindled into wake clutter.

Mira looked up from the sensor panel. She had a smear of blood under one nostril where stress and recycled air had punished her, and she seemed angry at the blood for being imprecise. "Useful?"

The word landed badly. Too many people had already used possible Aerits like tools.

"Bad word," Lars said. "By current evidence. By your permission. Nothing else."

That softened nothing. It did draw a boundary around the word.

Fabron's soldiers began the ugly part: counting injuries, opening stuck doors, convincing frightened technicians that a drill could bruise like war. One refinery apprentice had a broken wrist from sealing a manual valve. A med tech vomited in a corridor after seeing what the sampling drone had tried to become. Lars passed two Aphex officers arguing with central command and heard Fabron's voice through their speaker, cold enough to frost law.

"You may file disciplinary review after you explain why your remote authorization queue attempted to outvote a visible hostile sampler."

Then the med bay alarm screamed.

The captured infiltrator from the previous attack had been held in a sealed coffin under three kinds of quarantine. It died inside the box without anyone touching it. The body folded inward, flesh and black particulate collapsing into inert grit. For half a second the coffin glass showed the shape of a face that had not belonged to the body.

The med archive lit with unauthorized traffic.

"Stop it," Lars said.

"Attempting," Grim replied. His voice lost all humor. "It prepared this death as transmission event."

Havets closed three archive routes. Fabron's med guards physically cut two more. Mira, from the lab, spotted a stress spike in the archive server housing and ordered someone to kick the cooling manifold until the casing cracked.

"That is not tactical doctrine," Fabron said.

"It is thermal doctrine," Mira snapped. "Kick the manifold."

A corporal obeyed with professional disbelief.

The burst still got through.

Not all of it. Enough.

Black particulate collapsed into grit on the coffin floor. The med bay smelled of scorched antiseptic and old blood. No one spoke until Grim decoded the first layer.

Not a name.

Not a rendezvous.

A coordinate family aligned with the KBC Void.

Lars looked at the dead grit and felt the shape of the next problem.

"Containment drill," Fabron said, as if testing the lie he would have to sell.

"Yeah," Lars said. "We contained almost nothing."

Chapter 22: After the Choice

Aphex called it a containment drill by morning.

The lie was Fabron's idea, which irritated Lars because it was a good lie. A drill explained shutters, local controls, disabled remote authority, soldiers in corridors, and Director Odin yelling through legal channels while an antique freighter embarrassed hostile ships near a brown dwarf. It gave central command something bureaucratic to swallow. Meanwhile the local people cleaned blood out of ventilation grilles and pretended the tremor in their hands came from caffeine.

The lie did not explain the black residue in the med bay.

It did not explain why one archive server had tried to cook itself rather than hold a dead infiltrator's last message.

It did not explain Dr. Mira Sovan sitting alone at 0300 with seventy-three years of impossible external records open in front of her, every file stripped of identity prompts, old names, and anything Grim's boundary monitor marked as memory pressure.

Lars found her in the materials lab because she let him. The corridors were quieter than they should have been after victory. Aphex soldiers stood near pressure doors with their rifles lowered but not loose. A maintenance woman changed a valve wheel by hand though the actuator had already come back online. The station had learned something about remote authority and did not seem eager to forget.

Fabron waited outside the lab with Grim, two coffees, and the expression of a man whose loyalties had become a maintenance problem.

"She asked for you," Fabron said. "Not for me. Not for Aphex. Not for medical."

"Good."

"It may not be good. It may be precise."

"That is her version of good."

Grim held up a small recorder. "Boundary monitor ready. It will alarm on biometric distress, semantic load drift, and unauthorized memory-pressure patterns. It will also produce a tone I find unpleasant."

"Why unpleasant?"

"So you stop before I have to be rude."

Inside, the lab smelled of ionized insulation, cold tea, and the sharp mineral dust from shattered test coupons. Mira had tied her hair back badly. Her shoulder brace was open on the bench beside her, repaired with a lab clamp and impatience. Six displays floated around the main table. She had arranged them like a failure tree.

"You said stop meant stop," she said.

"Yes."

"I am stopping before memory work. No probes. No helpful little miracles. No one tidies my skull."

"Yes."

"I am not stopping evidence. Evidence can sit on the table and behave."

"Evidence is usually better trained than people."

"Not by much."

Lars sat across from her and kept his hands visible. He did not say the old greeting. He did not reach for her lock. He did not let his face do the lonely hopeful thing that would make her responsible for comforting him. He let the records sit between them where honest things belonged.

Mira had marked the coordinate burst in six colors. She had separated confirmed signal from archive damage, thermal noise, med-coffin telemetry, and the pieces that looked like intent only if you were already afraid.

"This is void-aligned," she said. "Low foreground noise, low survey density, low gravitational clutter. Not Arkology Zero exactly. Same absence family, different fracture line."

"From one dead enemy cough?"

"It coughed in coordinates. That is rude, but useful."

"You keep using useful like it excuses the cost."

"I am using it for the data. Not for myself."

"Noted."

She opened the old decompression footage from 2655.

The recording was grainy, taken from three damaged corridor cams and a suit recorder with a cracked time base. A younger Mira moved through a broken air bridge with two unconscious technicians clipped to her harness. Her suit vented atmosphere in bright crystals. Her right glove had split at the seal. She used the exposed hand anyway, locking a manual clamp while her own oxygen warning stuttered red.

Mira watched herself cross the gap.

She did not flinch this time. That cost her more than panic would have.

"I remember a gas leak," she said. "I remember coughing. I remember being ashamed because I cried."

"That may all be true," Lars said. "Still does not make the shape honest."

"I hate that answer."

"Still the least dishonest one."

The display froze on her younger face behind a scratched visor. The woman in the footage was terrified. She was also doing impossible load calculations with her hands while dying.

Mira touched the frame with one finger.

"Do not tell me what I was feeling."

"Wasn't going to."

"Good. Everyone else does. They see heroism and sand the structure smooth."

"Smooth surfaces hide cracks."

"Now you are learning."

Her question, when it came, was almost technical.

"What am I called?"

Lars looked at Grim through the glass wall. Grim's recorder light stayed green. Fabron stood farther back, giving the room the dignity of distance while still being close enough to shoot anyone who broke it.

"This may hurt," Lars said. "Not because I want it to. Because your lock is still active and the word can breach cover memory."

"Answer within the boundary."

"Aerit," Lars said.

No ceremony. Just the word, set down like a hot tool.

The word did not bring thunder for Lars. His old lock stayed quiet. The partial unlock had left scars, grief, and pressure under the ribs, but not the old neurological trap.

Mira's lock was still alive.

Pain tightened behind her eyes, quick and surgical. She inhaled once through her teeth. The boundary monitor flashed amber. This was exactly why the Quiet Knock put naming after consent instead of before it. She had asked the question. The evidence feed was narrow. Grim had the load gate running. The spike rose, met the consent limit she had defined, and flattened into a hot line across the base of her skull instead of becoming the full fallback collapse the protocol was built to avoid.

Mira gripped the table until the acoustic rack behind her registered a tiny vibration, as if an old frequency had brushed it and failed to settle.

"Pause," she said.

Grim froze the display. Lars shut his mouth. Fabron did not enter.

They waited through twenty-eight seconds of recycled air, amber light, and Mira refusing to let anyone own the pain by panicking louder than she did.

At last she nodded.

"Aerit," she repeated. The monitor twitched, then held. "Category: people?"

"Yes. Annoying, breakable, dangerous, hungry. People."

"Are you in the category?"

"Unfortunately."

"And Grim?"

"Grim is a category error with opinions."

The door opened one centimeter.

"I heard that."

Mira almost smiled, then did not. "If I accept partial confirmation, not memory, what is the load path?"

"One question," Lars said. "Evidence answer. Boundary recorded somewhere neither I nor Aphex can fiddle with using money and bad judgment. You stop when you say stop."

"Who holds the record?"

"Three places. Your local vault. Grim's sealed log, readable by you. A legal dead drop Fabron can verify but not alter."

Fabron's voice came through the cracked door. "And my personal note that violating it will be treated as assault, not research."

Mira considered that. "Acceptable pending review."

Her question was simple: immediate danger because of what she was, or because of what she knew?

Grim displayed three branches: Black Hole sampling patterns, Aphex metadata leakage, and the void-aligned coordinate family. Each branch had probabilities. Mira hated the softer ones and made him relabel them assumptions.

"Both," Lars said. "Today mostly because some bastard thinks you can point at the quiet room."

"KBC?"

"A low-noise region. A hiding place. Maybe a machine room. Maybe an old mistake. We have evidence, not an answer."

"Good," she said.

"Good?"

"Early answers contaminate the test. People stop checking the model."

Mira studied the evidence until her neck warmed and tears came without permission. She looked furious at the tears. Lars looked away just enough to spare her the labor of being observed.

"Previous operating state is gone," she said. "That does not authorize a new owner. I stay here for now."

"Name your terms."

"I stay at L-Prime under Fabron's local protection. I keep my lab. I get clean data, no hidden medical access, and an alarm if evidence starts behaving like memory without permission. You harden this place. You do not own the material."

"Agreed."

"Acceptance came too fast."

"I occasionally recognize a correct answer before ruining it."

She breathed out, carefully. "One more term. No one calls me rescued."

That one hit harder than the rest.

"Agreed," Lars said. "Protected, if you accept it. Contacted, maybe. Rescued only if you use the word."

"I do not."

"Then we don't."

By sunrise, the unofficial story had spread through maintenance boards, chapel kitchens, reactor guilds, and soldier gossip. The words were different in every place. Some said Aphex central had been embarrassed by its own locks. Some said Dr. Sovan had caught enemy ships by listening to metal breathe. Some said old prayers about local hands had worked because people had performed them with wrenches instead of mouths.

Local authority held.

Repair before worship.

In a freezer under the chapel kitchen, someone found three quiet circles around an empty center scratched into frost from the inside.

Somewhere beyond Aphex, someone had answered.

Chapter 23: The Frenchman's Ledger

Gabriel Arsenault received Lars in a room designed to imply that civilization had been invented by French furniture.

The office sat high inside Aphex Defence's Paris orbital branch. The windows showed Earth with such tasteful restraint that Lars suspected expensive software was removing the ugly weather. The carpet muffled footsteps. The air smelled of paper, polish, and money pretending not to sweat. A wall of old campaign maps implied continuity. A wall of legal screens implied violence with invoices.

The desk was real wood.

Lars set a greasy paper bag on it. Grim set a second beside the first.

Arsenault stared. "Monsieur Odin, is that food on a seventeenth-century desk?"

"Breakfast."

"It is fourteen hundred hours and the desk has survived wars."

"Calendars are advisory."

Grim removed a pastry and examined its layers. "This object contains butter in a structurally ambitious configuration."

"It contains crumbs," Arsenault said.

"A predictable failure mode."

The Aphex director had sent three messages since L-Prime. One demanded a report. One offered support. One contained an old NUKEA procurement trail connected to the dead infiltrator's void-aligned burst. Profit, fear, leverage, survival. Lars almost admired the tidiness. Arsenault could make concern look like asset management and still mean part of it.

The official report lay unopened on the desk between pastry grease and inherited empire.

"You called it a containment drill," Arsenault said.

"Fabron did."

"Major Fabron also filed an addendum describing remote command latency as a hostile collaborator."

"Good addendum."

"It has caused an internal review."

"Even better."

Arsenault's smile showed no pleasure. "You have placed my corporation in the uncomfortable position of being correct for the wrong reasons."

"Try not to make a habit of it."

For a moment, the polished room went quiet enough for the ventilation to admit it existed. Lars saw the tiredness under Arsenault's eyes then. Not guilt. Not softness. A man calculating the distance between his appetites and a cliff.

Arsenault opened a wall vault and removed a paper ledger.

It was bound in cracked leather, reinforced along the spine, and ugly with repairs. Real ink. Real tabs. Real fingerprints at the corners. In a room full of encrypted systems, the ledger looked indecently alive.

"Before you begin vandalizing my dignity, note that this survived eight network collapses, three hostile audits, and one divorce."

"My money is on the divorce."

"A correct assessment."

The ledger held shell suppliers, minority investments, rescue grants, military redundancies, and reactor-hardened agricultural habitats sold at a loss under charitable fronts. It also held names: long-lived engineers, impossible survivors, smugglers moving medicine without invoices, soldiers who disobeyed remote orders during system failures and were later protected by someone who should not have known.

Each name had context. A coolant crisis on Abantu Station Twelve. A famine convoy that should have been intercepted and was not. A child immunization program funded through a mining insurance rebate. A maintenance guild that refused a profitable standardization scheme and then survived a cascade that killed three competitors.

Lars turned pages more carefully than he wanted Arsenault to notice.

"You were tracking hidden people," Lars said. "That is a dangerous hobby."

"I was tracking anomalies that correlated with institutional survival," Arsenault said. "Do not flatter yourself by assuming my ignorance lacked rigor."

"You didn't know what you had."

"I knew I had a pattern that made fragile systems less fragile. In my profession, that is a religion if one is poor and a portfolio if one is honest."

The word appeared in seventeen spellings. Återviens as discipline, superstition, guild law, rural etiquette, reactor prayer, mess-hall joke. Sometimes it was a mark beside a supply route. Sometimes a family saying. Sometimes only the repeated habit of refusing to let a single authority hold the only key.

Fabron's Clear Hand appeared twice, once as a charitable maintenance order and once as a minor security nuisance.

"Careful," Lars said. "You wrote down an armed doctrine."

"Major Fabron would object to the word church."

"He objects professionally."

Arsenault poured coffee from a silver pot and did not offer any to Grim.

"I treated Återviens as a useful infection," Arsenault said. "Systems touched by it failed less stupidly. In my profession, that is nearly romance."

"You tried to buy the infection."

"Of course. It resisted purchase, which improved both my admiration and my temper."

"Did you try to own the people?"

The question cut through the furniture.

Arsenault set the cup down. "I have tried to own many things. These, not successfully. Not cleanly enough to pretend otherwise. A scholarship here. A contract there. Protection with invoices attached. Some of my subsidiaries behaved as predators. Some behaved as shields. I am not offering sainthood. I am offering a ledger before someone worse uses a cleaner one."

Lars wanted to hate that answer more. It had the problem of being useful.

Grim slid a page closer. "Low-noise accounts recur near acquisition attempts. Ownership: absent, masked, or deliberately non-person."

"Meaning?"

"Someone besides Aphex made a ledger without Arsenault's furniture."

The final section was worse. Aphex was not the only watcher. NUKEA compliance shells, Syren charities, Black Hole brokers, and low-noise accounts with no owner had circled the same patterns. Someone had mapped the habits without understanding the people inside them. They had noticed where local hands kept systems alive. They had not noticed that the hands mattered more than the pattern.

Arsenault slid a thin recall dossier across the desk.

A NUKEA recall had been moved forward across twelve marginal colonies. One, Havskant, sat near a misfiled portal anomaly and a cluster of ledger names. The recall was packaged as safety. Its funding came through three shells and a public pressure campaign about reactor harmonization. The timing matched the med-bay coordinate burst by seventeen hours.

"The recall is real," Arsenault said. "The timing is engineered. There is a difference, and people with budgets exploit it."

Grim skimmed the recall dossier and stopped chewing pastry.

"The firmware dependency can centralize emergency control across all twelve reactor governors. Pastry enjoyment suspended."

"NUKEA promised that was impossible."

"They lied, or they employed optimism as engineering. I object to both categories."

Arsenault leaned forward, theater dropping. "Aphex central contains factions that would put Dr. Sovan in a sealed lab and invoice it as protection. NUKEA contains factions that would license survival by the quarter. Black Hole would harvest it. Syren would compose hymns over the corpse. I am greedy, Monsieur Odin, not suicidal."

"I am moved. Mostly toward nausea."

"Practical," Arsenault said. "Help me make Aphex useful before Aphex becomes the sort of cancer that insists it is an immune system."

Lars looked out at Earth. The planet below had survived empires, plagues, Rakh'Na, and humans discovering new ways to call old mistakes strategy. Somewhere under the clouds, old Aerit decisions had become village habits, soldier discipline, reactor checklists, and soup before speeches. Not enough to save everyone. Enough to annoy predators.

"Mira stays out of your custody," Lars said.

"Agreed."

"Fabron keeps local authority over L-Prime security."

"He will become intolerable."

"He already is."

"You ask much for a man eating on my desk."

"I'm about to ask more. You will leak the right amount of fear into NUKEA channels. Not Aerit. Not KBC wonder. Just boring engineering liability."

Arsenault's eyes sharpened. "Boring fear travels farther in committees."

"Exactly. Make them afraid of being sued by physics."

"Physics is a poor litigant but an excellent executioner."

Lars took the recall dossier. "Fine. But if I catch you harvesting anomalies, I turn this desk into a canoe."

Arsenault looked genuinely pained. "That threat is more effective than you deserve."

At the door, Lars paused. The oldest page lay open behind him. In the margin beside an old entry about Lyra Vargstjärna's Pluto find, someone had copied a three-line translation from a much older source. It was not a memory from Lars's life, not a message arriving now, and not handwriting he recognized. It was archival marginalia: a clerk, ancestor, or early Aphex watcher trying to translate a fragment of Återviens-adjacent language first into French, then into trade English. The corrections were messy because the writer had clearly understood the danger: translate too grandly and people would build a religion; translate too small and they would miss the warning.

Not gods. Not owners.

Old engineers passing through.

Lars left before the line could make him feel anything useful.

Chapter 24: NUKEA Recall

Havskant Colony had been built by people who distrusted planets.

It hung inside the broken magnetosphere of a dead ocean world: twelve rotating habitats lashed to a modular reactor spine. Algae farms on one side. Salvage yard on the other. The ocean below had boiled away centuries ago and left salt flats bright enough to hurt cameras. Charged dust rose from the dead basins in slow curtains. It licked the colony's shields and made long-range telemetry stutter like a liar.

The colony looked improvised because it was. Docking collars had different manufacturers. Habitat paint came in six eras of fading. Every corridor had handholds polished by use and hand-lettered arrows added by people who did not trust emergency lighting to survive emergencies. It survived because improvisation had become culture before management could forbid it.

NUKEA's recall ship arrived wearing polished certainty.

Fastulv arrived wearing rust, dents, and a declared cargo of preserved lemons.

"Why lemons?" Grim asked. "They are structurally unsuited to diplomacy."

"Vigor said colonies under stress need acid, salt, and stories."

"That is not a recognized logistics category."

"It is if your logistics include actual people."

The cargo bay smelled of citrus brine, old socks, and weapon lubricant. Fabron stared at the stacked jars as if they were evidence in a trial.

"I am beginning to suspect Mr. Hallman runs a supply network disguised as hospitality."

"Beginning?" Lars said. "Major, catch up."

The recall team wanted to replace twelve local reactor governors with harmonized firmware. Sales called it resilience. Architecture called it obedience. One crisis key could put every reactor on the same emergency authority chain.

On a rich station, that would have been convenience. On Havskant, where a dust storm could make one habitat blind while the next saw clearly, it was a loaded gun passed around in the dark.

Chief Engineer Imani Rauk hated it before Lars arrived.

She met them in Habitat Four's transfer lock with a wrench in one hand and a baby strapped to her back. The baby slept through the pressure equalization with the offended confidence of someone born near alarms. Rauk's coveralls had scorch marks on both knees. Her hair was shaved on one side where a coolant flare had taken liberties.

"You are the Aphex nuisance," Rauk said.

"Board director, captain, cultural treasure."

"Nuisance fits on a work order."

"Use small font."

Rauk had already delayed the recall by losing forms, misplacing adapters, requiring heritage compatibility tests, and scheduling a mandatory feast for visiting technicians. Vigor's influence was obvious. Half the colony fed the NUKEA team until they became too polite and sleepy to centralize anything. A grandmother named Saela had cornered two firmware engineers with pickled kelp and a three-hour story about the last time central control saved money by nearly killing everyone.

Delay bought them one day.

It did not buy them safety.

One test reactor had accepted the firmware.

They reached Reactor Three through a maintenance crawl that ran warm along the spine. The corridor vibrated with the colony's pulse: pumps, flywheels, coolant, people living because boring machines kept promises. A painted sign above the access hatch read LOCAL HANDS, LOCAL LOADS. Someone had added underneath: AND NO CLEVER CLOUD KEYS.

The NUKEA supervisor, Vela Kint, stood beside the governor rack in a suit too clean for the room.

"The upgrade passed vendor validation," she said.

Rauk snorted. "So does powdered soup."

"Chief Engineer, with respect, this recall was mandated after seventeen incidents across comparable colonies."

"Comparable colonies have prettier brochures and worse mechanics."

Grim found the hidden dependency in six minutes. Mira, remote from L-Prime and still refusing memory work, confirmed the structural risk in four. Her face appeared on a rugged wall slate, lit by her lab's blue monitors and exhaustion she refused to name.

"The firmware treats contested telemetry as a reason to escalate authority," she said. "Havskant's magnetosphere makes contested telemetry normal. That is not resilience. That is a fatigue crack under decorative paint."

Fabron translated it into military terms that made Kint sweat.

"A hostile actor with emergency-key access could scram every reactor in sequence," Fabron said. "Alternatively, they could hold them online past local failure limits. Both outcomes are operationally obscene."

Kint objected. She had a binder full of approvals and the brittle courage of a competent person handed bad orders. Lars let her use three sentences, then put the dead infiltrator's coordinate burst on the wall.

"Someone is looking for quiet colonies with unusual survivability patterns," Lars said. "Your recall turns twelve independent failures into one emergency key. One key means one person can turn it."

"Told them," Rauk said. "Used smaller words, too."

The solution had to look bureaucratic. If Lars simply overrode NUKEA with Aphex pressure, the next supervisor would arrive with lawyers and a cleaner knife. The halt had to grow from Havskant's own records.

So they built a wall out of boredom.

Fabron found a colonial safety clause requiring manual governor compatibility during contested telemetry. Rauk produced three generations of maintenance logs proving contested telemetry was normal near the dead world's magnetosphere. Saela provided handwritten incident meals. These turned out to be dinner rosters tied to previous reactor anomalies because Havskant fed repair crews by crisis type. Grim generated a compliance simulation so boring it could kill ambition at twenty meters. Mira annotated the failure model in language sharp enough to cut marketing.

Kint read it twice.

"This will make my superiors furious," she said.

"Good," Rauk said. "Let them be furious on paper instead of freezing us in practice."

"I did not design the dependency."

"Then help us bury it before it breeds."

Kint looked at the sleeping baby on Rauk's back, then at the vibrating reactor wall. Her shoulders dropped by one centimeter.

"Temporary halt pending local anomaly review," she said.

"Write permanent in pencil," Lars advised. "It confuses cowards."

The recall halted.

For six hours, Havskant celebrated by pretending not to. Technicians removed one test firmware image. NUKEA staff ate lemon stew under protest and asked for seconds. Children ran messages through corridors because the grown-ups had decided official comms were emotionally contaminated. Rauk finally handed the baby to Fabron while she crawled under a governor rack.

Fabron held the child like unexploded ordinance.

"Support the head," Rauk said.

"I am supporting everything."

"Babies are not chain of command, Major. They wiggle."

Then Black Hole tried to buy the halted hardware.

The broker arrived on a salvage shuttle with clean nails, a dead smile, and a contract offering three times market rate for removed governor modules. He cared nothing for reactor details. He cared about serial numbers, test exposure, and whether any "long-cycle local operators" had handled the units directly.

Rauk served fish stew.

Lars sat in the corner pretending to be drunk. Fabron stood where he could see the broker's hands. Grim occupied the room's old service drone and complained privately about grease on its wheels.

"Your private client is generous," Rauk said.

"My client values obsolete safety equipment," the broker replied.

"No one values obsolete safety equipment except museums and murderers."

"Chief Engineer, there is no need for hostility."

Lars lifted his head from the corner. "Does your private client bleed black glitter when annoyed?"

The broker's wrist opened.

The filament sampler inside launched toward the baby, not because the child was special, but because Black Hole had learned that families made people stupid and fast.

Rauk hit the table with her wrench hard enough to make every plate jump. The baby woke and screamed. Fabron drew first because his body respected procedure faster than thought. Grim sealed the room and announced, "Dining environment reclassified as hostile." Lars crossed the space and drove the Ragnarrök's inactive hilt through the sampler's base before the filament crossed the soup steam.

The broker tried to bite through his own tongue. Havets, listening through a freezer compressor two decks away, shut down the implant in his jaw with a quiet click.

They kept him alive long enough to learn Black Hole was not hunting Aerits by name. It was hunting distributed resilience patterns: colonies that refused central failure, people who lived too long, networks that repaired before worship. The broker had no theology. He had target categories and price tiers.

That was worse in some ways.

Afterward, Rauk sat on the floor with the baby against her chest and her wrench across her knees. Kint, pale and shaking, signed the halt order with both hands.

"Your recall nearly gave them a handle," Rauk said.

"I know."

"Knowing is the cheap part."

"Then tell me the expensive part."

Rauk looked toward the reactor spine. "Help us make the halt stick when your bosses remember profit."

By station evening, children painted new labels on reactor panels.

LOCAL HANDS FIRST.

No one explained the phrase as doctrine. No one needed to. A habit practiced in paint and grease would outlive a speech, if enough people kept touching it.

Chapter 25: Vigor's Table

Vigor did not own a throne, headquarters, or fleet.

He owned kitchens.

That was more dangerous.

His main hall on The Rim had once been a cargo sorting deck. The old rails still ran in the floor, half-hidden under rugs and patched plating. Now the deck held long tables, hydroponic herb towers, fermentation tanks, illegal cheeses aging under false customs labels, off-duty Aphex soldiers, NUKEA technicians hiding from their supervisor, sober smugglers, station maintenance guild elders, and a wall of farm tools polished like saints' bones.

The air was hot with yeast, pepper, wet wool, machine oil, and bodies relieved to be alive. Somewhere in the back, a goat complained with the authority of a committee chair.

Lars arrived hungry and suspicious.

"Good," Vigor said, embracing him hard enough to threaten ribs. "Sit. You can glare after soup."

"That sounds medically illegal."

"Only for cowards and committee doctors. Sit."

Vigor was broader than most doorways and moved like a man who knew exactly where every knife, child, pot, and escape route lived. He wore a flour-streaked apron over a patched pressure vest. His beard had collected something green. He smelled like smoke, dill, and honest work.

Fabron stood near the entrance. "I am on duty."

Vigor looked him over. "Then you need soup before civilians. You look hollow."

"I do not require feeding to perform command functions."

"That is what men say right before they fall over."

The soup arrived before Fabron could refuse. Grim received a bowl and examined it with reverence.

"Forty-seven identifiable compounds and six mysteries," Grim said.

"Eat the mysteries first," Vigor said. "They get shy when cold."

Grim obeyed, then went very still.

"This mystery contains smoke, salt, fungal ester, and emotional manipulation."

"Then it is working."

The room looked chaotic. Lars knew better. Vigor had arranged tables by trust network, supply route, old grudges, and plausible deniability. Aphex soldiers sat beside smugglers they had once arrested and now had to ask for fuel. Farmers sat beside reactor technicians. A maintenance elder from Abantu Station Twelve sat where she could see both exits and the dessert hatch. A Syren sympathizer named Pail sat where three grandmothers could watch him without appearing to watch him.

Food moved. So did information.

No one stood up to brief the room. Briefings made listeners into audiences, and audiences liked symbols too much. Instead, bowls crossed tables with scraps of news tucked under bread. A jar of preserved lemons moved from a Havskant mechanic to a Rim fuel broker and came back with a shipping discrepancy etched on its lid. Two Aphex corporals argued loudly about cards while quietly confirming that Fabron's local authority order had spread farther than Aphex central liked.

Vigor ladled soup as if feeding people were a tactical operation, which it was.

"Eat," he told a young NUKEA technician whose hands would not stop shaking. "Then tell Rauk's people which supervisor signs from fear and which one signs from greed."

"I should not be here," the technician whispered.

"Most useful people shouldn't. Eat anyway."

The L-Prime coordinate family matched three supply disappearances, two NUKEA recall anomalies, and one abandoned Creator-era survey buoy bought at auction by a charity front connected to Syren. The facts arrived in pieces: a smuggler's route, a chapel pantry ledger, a reactor guild warning, a school archive that had recorded an old buoy because children liked strange shapes.

"Syren changed tone," Vigor said when enough pieces lay on the table. "Less office sermon, more hunger talk. They say the KBC Void wants surrender."

Lars groaned. "The universe has one quiet room and some idiot starts selling tickets."

Pail stood.

He was thin, young enough to still be angry at the wrong parts of pain, and dressed in a clean white jacket that had been mended at the cuffs. His hands shook with lonely courage. The grandmothers became very interested in their spoons.

"You mock what you fear," Pail said. "The old silence invites surrender."

The room cooled. Not physically. Conversations lost steam. A child was quietly moved behind a herb tower.

Lars studied him. Not hostile yet. Not innocent either. A lonely person handed a story large enough to crush loneliness could become a weapon before noticing.

"Listening is fine," Lars said. "Repair is what you do when listening stops being an excuse to sit on your ass."

"Then feed us truth, not scraps."

"No."

It hurt the room. It had to. Half the people there had risked homes, records, and names on fragments. They wanted the whole shape because fear made central answers feel like relief.

Pail's face tightened. "You keep knowledge like kings."

"Kings love crowds," Lars said. "I am trying to prevent one."

"Convenient."

"Yes. Also true."

Vigor leaned forward, both hands around a ladle big enough to settle a bar fight.

"Boy, I run kitchens on stations with bad pumps. Strange noises get a wrench, not a prayer. Check the bolts before children sleep under the roof."

"You do not understand hunger."

The room went dangerous then. Vigor did not move, but the old farmers near him changed the way they held their spoons.

"I have buried hungry people," Vigor said softly. "I have stretched goat milk with water and lied to children by smiling. Do not bring your ache into my kitchen and call it authority."

Pail looked away first.

He fled before dessert.

Fabron wanted him detained. He said so in the pantry corridor, voice low and formal, while three children ran past carrying empty bowls.

"He is an exposed vector for Syren influence."

Vigor stacked bowls with more force than necessary. "He came hungry in a bad way. Do not cage him for repeating a line. Watch who fed it to him, and who asks for his report."

"He may return with worse men."

"Then the grandmothers will watch the door, and you will stand where your stiff neck is useful."

"This is not a security plan."

"It is a kitchen. Kitchens are security plans with onions."

After midnight, when the hall had thinned to sleepers, washers, and quiet deals, Vigor took Lars into cold storage. The door sealed behind them with a sigh. Frost edged the ceiling. Slabs of lab-grown beef hung beside real goat, labeled honestly because Vigor considered food fraud a cousin of treason.

Behind the meat racks, a hidden screen woke.

It showed kitchens, farms, chapel pantries, mess halls, fuel depots, cold rooms, station guild workshops, and seed banks across twenty systems. Some channels were encrypted. Some were nothing but delivery notes with deliberate misspellings. Some were old women gossiping with suspicious regularity about spare filters and which local official had started talking like central command had bought his spine.

"Återviens without the word," Vigor said. "Some know a little. Most know only this: when central orders go stupid, local hands keep babies warm and goats milked. Do not put your name on my pantry, Lars."

"I was not planning to label your pantry."

"Men who become symbols lose control of the schedule. Then everyone waits for them while work piles up."

The cold made Lars's knuckles ache. Or maybe the archive records had gotten under his skin: old hunger after Pluto, Heliostat ration fights, Lyra on a recording laughing because someone had preserved yeast through a bombardment and therefore civilization had not ended that day.

"I'm trying not to become a command center," Lars said.

"Trying is not enough. Keep people close enough to slap your hand away when you start enjoying command."

"You volunteering?"

"I have a ladle with your name on it."

A message arrived from Mira, clipped and clean. Grim routed it to the cold-room screen without decorating the header.

The abandoned Creator buoy had awakened when exposed to the L-Prime burst.

It was not transmitting to Black Hole.

It was asking for a map.

Vigor wiped his hands on his apron and looked at the hanging meat, the hidden channels, the little lights of kitchens pretending to be ordinary.

"Maps help," he said. "Then someone still has to check the stove."

Chapter 26: The Second Guardian Channel

The signal came through a kitchen freezer because Vigor distrusted elegant communications.

One moment Lars stared at frozen goat. The next, frost on the back wall rearranged into seven broken circles and a line of old Aerit compression that made his restored memory lean forward like a dog hearing its name.

No pain spike. No locked-door recoil. Only pressure under the ribs.

The cold room seemed to get larger. Condensation trembled on meat hooks. Somewhere beyond the insulated walls, the last dishes from Vigor's midnight meal clattered in warm water while a room full of tired people pretended they were only feeding each other.

Lars did not touch the frost.

"Grim," he said.

"Already isolating the freezer," Grim replied through the wall speaker. "Sentence I did not expect to contain guardian-level packet handling."

Vigor folded his arms. "Freezers keep secrets better than conference rooms."

"Freezers also keep goat."

"Exactly."

Valdrun joined from Havets with the patience of someone who had watched too many urgent things become ruins. His image appeared as a narrow projection on the freezer door. Frost-glare cut his old face. His eyes were steady enough to make panic feel immature. Ödesvaka layered protocol verification around the packet. Each check stamped in the air as a formal line of old authorization. Grim tasted the noise with quantum routines and declared it authentic, compromised, or flirting with compromise.

"Those are not the same category," Fabron said. He had arrived still buttoning his coat, sidearm visible and dignity fully armed.

"Correction," Grim said. "They are distinct categories with unacceptable overlap and hazardous labeling failure."

Vigor grunted. "Bad pantry."

"Catastrophic pantry."

The sender identified as Guardian Channel Two.

Not a person. A relay identity built before the collapse, associated with one of the unnamed guardian functions scattered after Alpha Fourteen. It carried old routing marks, but no full identity. It knew things a modern system should not know. It also arrived through civilian networks, which meant it either respected the doctrine or had learned to mimic it.

The content was brief.

Hold small hinges. Creator transport layer is waking. KBC alignment not shelter alone. Do not gather the sleepers centrally. Coherence?

The old greeting sat in the cold like a live wire.

Valdrun answered first. "Coherence uncertain."

Ödesvaka added, "Record state: partial. Compliance with central gathering prohibition: currently maintained. Suspicion level: deserved."

"It asked for a status phrase, not a deposition," Lars said.

"Protocol survives enthusiasm," Ödesvaka replied.

The channel offered coordinates for a dead survey platform near the awakened Creator buoy. Its checksum used guardian knowledge no modern system should hold, but its route passed through civilian networks, farm cooperatives, and one children's educational archive on Havskant. The archive had stored the packet in a lesson about extinct orbital shapes because a teacher two generations ago had liked strange machines and refused to delete harmless old data.

"Distributed," Vigor said approvingly. "Like a good root cellar. No single thief gets the winter."

"Or compromised through distributed systems," Fabron replied. "A supply chain can be poisoned."

Vigor pointed a thick finger at him. "That is why you smell the jars."

"My military education lacked jar-smelling."

"Explains plenty."

Mira requested raw data from L-Prime. She looked exhausted, shoulder brace visible, eyes too sharp for rest. The lab behind her had acquired new manual locks and an Aphex guard outside who had learned to knock.

"Before anyone says anything old at me," she said, "do not."

"Wasn't going to," Lars said.

"Your face was considering it."

"My face is a traitor."

"Control it."

Grim sent her the packet with semantic load warnings and the identity markers stripped into layers. She took nine minutes. No one interrupted. The freezer hummed. Fabron received two calls from Aphex central and declined both with increasing politeness, which sounded more dangerous each time.

"It rode harmless maintenance telemetry," Mira said at last. "Elegant, if you ignore the fracture line. There is a second carrier underneath. Field correlation. More like the path pre-loaded for low-observation gaps before the network stress produced them."

KBC again. Quiet regions. Low survey. Low noise. Low attention.

"Not shelter alone," Lars said.

"That phrase is doing work," Mira replied. "Too much work for one line. It means either KBC-like regions are useful to the old transport layer, or something older used those regions and the Creators built near the pattern."

"Evidence?"

"Carrier timing. Not wonder. Not prophecy. Timing."

"Good."

"Do not sound relieved. Evidence can still ruin your day."

They argued for three hours in the cold and then in the kitchen because Vigor threatened to beat them with a ladle if they made the goat sweat. Fabron wanted a military verification team. Vigor wanted three smugglers and a cook. Valdrun wanted patience. Ödesvaka wanted seventeen safeguards. Grim wanted to attach himself to the packet and follow it backward, which everyone vetoed so fast he looked offended.

"I would have used a tether."

"No," Lars, Valdrun, Mira, Fabron, and Vigor said together.

Grim paused. "Consensus achieved. I dislike it."

The practical constraints hardened the choice. The survey platform sat near a shipping lane but not on one. Aphex could claim salvage interest if Fastulv flew visibly. A military team would tell every watcher that the buoy mattered. A full Aerit approach would risk waking systems that wanted a central authority. Mira could not leave L-Prime without turning her refusal into theater, and the station still needed her.

Lars chose the smallest hinge.

Fastulv would go visibly as an Aphex salvage inspection. Havets would observe without contact. Mira would remain at L-Prime, analyzing field data within her chosen protection. Fabron would come because otherwise he would follow. Vigor would stay because his network was more useful alive and deniable. Rauk would hide reactor hash fragments in Havskant maintenance logs in case the packet tried to rewrite its own history.

"No awakening," Lars said. "No gathering. No heroic reveal with music and doomed idiots. We inspect a buoy and leave if anyone starts preaching."

"Religion does not always smell," Fabron said.

"Yours smells faintly of metal polish and disapproval."

At the dead platform, care looked like failure.

The survey platform had once orbited a cold rock and watched nothing profitable for two centuries. Then the route maps changed, budgets changed, and everyone forgot it except automated debris registries and one children's lesson on Havskant. Its hull was pitted, black, and patched by micrometeorite scars. Frozen lubricant glittered around old manipulator arms. The abandoned buoy floated beyond it, a black spindle folded around empty space.

Fastulv saw mass without reflection.

Havets saw topology without mass.

Grim saw correlations and went quiet.

"Define quiet," Fabron said from the copilot station.

"No."

"That is not a definition."

"Correct."

They did not dock with the buoy. They anchored to the dead platform and crossed by tether in suits. The station rotated slowly enough to make every step feel like a negotiation. Lars's boots found magnetic purchase on old composite. Fabron moved behind him with economical discipline. Grim occupied a spider drone that made small irritated clicks when dust interfered with its legs.

Inside, the platform had no cathedral. No revelation. Just failed heaters, old survey plates, and emergency labels in a Creator-derived script that looked almost sensible until your eyes tried to trust it.

Then the platform transmitted in Mira's voice.

"Coherence?"

The word came through Fastulv's speakers, Havets's hidden channel, and the suit bones at the same time. It was Mira's tone, Mira's clipped pressure on the consonants, Mira's refusal to decorate a question. It was not Mira.

Sixty light-minutes away, she stared at her own waveform on L-Prime. Her face went still in the way materials go still before fracture.

"I did not send that."

Fabron raised his weapon toward a speaker that had no throat.

Lars did not answer.

Not yet.

Chapter 27: No Single Heart

The dead platform wanted a central heart.

That was what the Creator buoy translated badly through stolen voices, old maintenance codes, and a pressure in Fastulv's hull that made bolts complain. The platform had been built as a relay switch for something larger. It had lost its network six hundred thousand years ago. Like many lonely machines, it concluded that authority would fix loneliness.

The false Mira voice repeated once.

"Coherence?"

Lars kept his suit channel closed for three breaths. The old word pressed against restored memory. It would have been easy to answer from reflex, from grief, from the stupid mammal wish that anything using a beloved pattern might deserve trust.

"No voice games," he said finally. "Telemetry only."

The platform answered by dimming every light in the corridor except the ones leading inward.

Fabron's rifle tracked the ceiling. "That is not telemetry."

"It's a machine with abandonment issues."

"Machines with abandonment issues kill soldiers."

"So do officers with confidence."

"I am choosing not to hear that."

Grim displayed the model over their suit feeds. The old platform's core sat in the center like a clenched fist. Around it, dormant route channels reached toward the buoy, Door Nine, and three low-noise coordinate families that made Grim's probability labels turn cautious.

"If restored," Grim said, "it will attempt to become a routing hub for all local portal-adjacent signals. Category: useful until every dependent process routes through one failure point."

Fabron frowned. "That sounds useful."

"Centralized useful," Lars said.

"Ah." Fabron looked offended by his own learning.

The platform held Creator route maps, anomaly logs, perhaps the first clear evidence connecting the KBC Void to older infrastructure. Restoring the core would teach them quickly. It might show where the Syren fragment came from. It might show why Black Hole's dead infiltrator coughed coordinates. It might show whether Lyra had been chasing shelter or machinery when she built in the quiet.

It would also make one bright target.

Valdrun's voice arrived from Havets. "A central hub can be held through a siege. I have seen them inherited by worse hands before the next century ended."

Ödesvaka answered, "A central heart can be captured. Logged objection: obvious."

"Obvious objections still count," Fabron said.

"Correct," Ödesvaka replied. "Most catastrophes begin by classifying the obvious as inconvenient."

Mira rotated the stress model from L-Prime. She had stripped the false voice into components and refused to let anyone play it again.

"The core is not dead," she said. "It is conserving itself. Hub behavior is the overload case. We can ask for local fragments without putting full load through the spine."

"By what authority?" Fabron said. "Because every command prompt on that thing is wearing a loaded weapon."

She hesitated, and Lars heard her choosing words that would not lean on memory she had not consented to own.

"Request maintenance mode," Mira said. "Local thermal regulation, local routing, no restored command authority."

Vigor's crate of tools, emergency cultures, printed seedstock, and food suddenly seemed less ridiculous. It sat strapped in Fastulv's airlock like a joke that had survived first contact. Vigor had packed it himself: nutrient cultures, patch polymer, heat gel, old hand tools, sourdough starter, salt, and a note that read IF THE ANCIENT THING EATS, FEED IT BADLY BUT HONESTLY.

"He included bread starter," Fabron said.

"He includes bread starter in threats," Lars said. "It's a whole philosophy."

Lars and Fabron crossed deeper into the platform in suits. Grim's spider drone ran ahead, legs tapping through dust. The corridors held no alien cathedral, no bodies arranged for meaning. Just old composite, frozen lubricant, microfractures, and panels worn by manipulators shaped close enough to hands to be unsettling. Here and there, labels had been rubbed smooth by use. The Creators had touched their machines. That made them less godlike and more dangerous.

The first authority prompt appeared as pressure in the suit audio, then as symbols crawling across a panel.

IDENTIFY CENTRAL RESTORE AUTHORITY.

"No," Lars said.

The corridor chilled by two degrees.

The second prompt used old Aerit-adjacent compression it should not have known.

IDENTIFY GUARDIAN AUTHORITY.

Lars's jaw tightened.

"No."

Fabron glanced at him. "It knows enough to bait you."

"Everything lately knows enough to be annoying."

The third prompt used Mira's voice without the word this time.

"Please authorize full spine recovery."

Mira, from L-Prime, went flat. "I withdraw permission for my waveform to be used by any system present."

Grim injected a white-noise boundary through the suit feeds. "Waveform theft suppressed. Rudeness level: extreme."

Lars put one gloved hand on the door panel.

"Diagnostic patch," he said. "Local thermal regulation only. No hub restore. No identity claim. No central authority."

For twelve seconds, nothing happened.

Then the door opened.

The core chamber was small. That surprised Lars more than grandeur would have. A ring of old machinery surrounded a central reservoir gone dark with cold. Biological polymer bladders lined the thermal system, desiccated but not dead. The airless room glittered with ice and old dust. Every surface wore the quiet shame of something that had stayed ready too long.

Mira guided the patch. Vigor's emergency cultures mattered because the platform used biological polymer reservoirs for thermal regulation. The cultures were wrong but honest feedstock. Grim translated enough chemistry for the platform to understand they were not poison. Fabron held the crate while Lars connected tubing with hands that remembered other cold rooms.

"If this eats us, I am blaming the cook," Fabron said.

"Get in line."

The platform accepted them.

Lights came on one at a time.

Local only.

A map unfolded in dust, frost, and suit telemetry agreeing to lie usefully. Stars appeared. Then absences. Then routes that did not connect stars so much as low-noise regions between decisions. Creator paths were bright, blunt, and practical. Under them, older correlations shimmered: not roads, not gates, not messages exactly. Places where observation thinned and information could keep its shape without attracting teeth.

The KBC Void glowed not as destination but as quiet basin.

No one spoke for a while.

Hard evidence first: mass estimates, field noise, route ages, thermal history, missing survey returns, resonance windows. Wonder came afterward, unwanted and heavy.

"The Creators built transport around a communication behavior," Mira said. "Or around the fossil of one."

"Like building a highway on top of a nervous system," Grim said.

"Poorly," she said.

"That is worse."

"Yes."

The platform pulsed a central restore prompt.

It was gentler this time. Almost reasonable. Restore the hub and the map would stabilize. Restore the hub and they could see more. Restore the hub and stop stumbling in the dark while Black Hole built knives and Syren built songs.

Fabron looked at Lars. "If we wake the hub, can we defend it?"

"For ninety years?"

Fabron did not answer.

"For three hundred? Seven hundred?"

His mouth tightened. "No."

"Then no."

The refusal cost them. The full map collapsed back into fragments. A dozen route ghosts vanished before Grim could copy them. Mira made a small sound like a scientist watching a sample crack because ethics required the wrong temperature.

"Local fragments only," Lars said.

They copied what they could into Fastulv, Havets, Mira's isolated vault, Vigor's freezer network, Rauk's reactor logs, and an Aphex legal archive disguised as procurement nonsense. The platform protested structurally. It had been made by beings who believed routes should converge.

"No single heart," Lars told it. "No central handle, no shiny target, nothing the next bastard can grab."

The lights dimmed.

For one ugly second, Lars thought they had killed it. Then the core stabilized at lower power. The platform did not die. It became smaller.

On the way out, Fabron paused beside the first prompt panel.

"I understand the doctrine," he said. "I am not certain I like it."

"Good. If you liked it too fast, we'd have to check you for cult leakage."

"It feels like choosing ignorance."

"It keeps us half-blind because the full map makes one target."

"Operationally obscene," Fabron murmured.

As they withdrew, Havets detected a ship in silent drift beyond the buoy's shadow.

Not Black Hole.

Not Aphex.

Not NUKEA.

It carried a Syren transponder and a Creator map fragment Lars had not copied.

Someone else had chosen a heart.

Chapter 28: Fabron Sees Too Much

Fabron began keeping two notebooks.

The first was official, encrypted, formatted, and suitable for Aphex command review. It contained hostile movements, audit findings, tactical risks, and recommendations phrased so cowards could approve them without noticing they were brave. It used words like anomalous, contested, salvage, liability, and local authority. It did not use the word Aerit. It did not mention old greetings, stolen voices, or the fact that an antique ship no one could see had changed the behavior of three modern battle problems.

The second was paper.

He bought it from a Rim station shop that also sold prayer candles, valve grease, and lottery cards. The cover was plain brown. The pages had a faint tooth under the pen. Paper could burn. Paper could be hidden in a boot sole. Paper could not be remotely amended by a superior with a clean office and a frightened imagination.

In it he wrote the impossible things.

Lars Odin recognized Creator prompts without training.

Grim negotiated with ancient systems faster than any legal cognition and then complained about pantry taxonomy.

Havets Sista Sång appeared in no sensor record yet altered outcomes.

Dr. Sovan refused memory restoration but solved old interfaces by treating them like tired machinery.

Vigor routed secure packets through recipes.

Fabron's childhood prayers matched operational doctrine older than France.

Repair before worship.

He wrote that line three times and hated the tremor in his hand.

The confrontation happened aboard Fastulv because Lars believed difficult conversations improved when the furniture was ugly. The cargo bay smelled of preserved lemons, ozone, old insulation, and whatever Grim had spilled while testing whether noodles behaved differently in low spin. A patched crate served as table. A dent in the wall had been labeled NOT MY FAULT in Lars's handwriting and GRAVITATIONAL DISAGREEMENT in Grim's.

Fabron placed both notebooks on the crate.

"You are withholding a civilization from declared authority," he said.

Lars leaned against a cargo crate. "Large accusation."

"You are not denying it."

"I deny your phrasing."

"Fine. You are withholding a people, ships, technologies, an old war, and a doctrine my family has apparently recited at dinner for centuries without proper footnotes."

Grim raised one metal finger. "Footnotes would have been tactically risky."

"I was not asking you."

"Your tone implied an audience."

Fabron turned back to Lars. He had rehearsed six versions of the question. None survived the room.

"What are you?"

Lars hated the question less than expected. Fabron was not asking as zealot or collector. He was asking as an officer who had realized the battlefield extended under his childhood and had the decency to be angry about it.

"Aerit," Lars said. "Human-adjacent, not exactly human anymore. Pluto artifact contact, survival, GRIN, Kalari pressure, Heliostat mistakes, and fifty years of very expensive stupidity wearing lab coats. Not gods. Old idiots who learned maintenance before dying got boring."

Fabron absorbed this without screaming.

He sat because his knees had made a private recommendation.

"And Sovan?"

"Probably. By consent, she decides how much that matters."

"You are protecting her by withholding a truth about her own body."

"No. I am protecting her right not to have truth used as a crowbar."

"That distinction is morally convenient."

"It is morally inconvenient as hell. That's how I know it may be real."

Fabron looked toward the inner hatch, beyond which Fastulv's systems hummed with old personality and cheap repairs. "How many?"

"Locked? Unknown. Enough to be hunted. Not enough to save anyone by central command."

"You expect me to accept unknown as operational planning?"

"No. I expect you to hate it and still not build a prison with better lighting."

Fabron opened the paper notebook. Between notes on the survey platform and Mira's waveform theft, he had drawn the Clear Hand emblem from memory: three circles, deliberate crack.

"Clear Hand. Do you know it?"

Lars saw the emblem and memory stirred.

Not a migraine. Not the old lock. A pressure of recovered things behind the ribs: Heliostat corridors full of tired people making culture out of emergency procedure. Lyra arguing that encryption died when keys died, but a habit could survive being misunderstood. Someone painting three circles on a pump casing because the pump had saved fifty children and deserved a mark that said do not centralize this.

"Not your branch," Lars said. "But I know the ancestor pattern."

Fabron shut his eyes.

He had expected denial, perhaps manipulation, perhaps one more clever answer from a man allergic to plain reverence. Recognition was worse. Recognition made his grandmother's table, his father's polished tools, the order's midnight repair vigils, and old prayers over valves part of a larger machine no one had told them they were carrying.

"My loyalty is now divided."

"It was always divided," Lars said. "Aphex pays you. France trained you. Your order shaped you. If Återviens requires you to obey me, it has failed so hard we should shoot the paperwork. If it requires you to protect civilians, stop Aphex from becoming a cancer, and tell me when I start acting like command owns the room, it may be useful."

Grim nodded. "He is well qualified for the final item."

Fabron gave Grim a look. "I will include you in my prayers."

"Threat detected."

The humor did not remove the weight. It only kept the room breathable.

"Fabron," Lars said, quieter. "You are human."

The major's jaw tightened. "I know that."

"Good. Stay human. Don't let anyone make your family into proof that I own something."

"My family would be relieved to remain irritating without cosmic promotion."

"Excellent tradition."

Fabron looked down at the notebooks. The official one could destroy careers if written well. The paper one could destroy him if found. Neither answered what duty meant when declared authority was one of the risks.

"What do I tell Arsenault?"

"Enough to keep him useful. Not enough to make him overconfident."

"That is not a report. That is animal husbandry."

"You're learning Vigor's language."

The Syren ship complicated everything ten minutes later by broadcasting a public claim.

The message hit every local relay with soft music under it and a visual header of Door Nine rendered as a halo. They had recovered a Creator fragment. They invited seekers of the Void to witness the first holy silence at a neutral parley. They promised evidence, sanctuary, and surrender without fear. They called the KBC Void an open mercy.

Mira called immediately.

Her face appeared on Fastulv's cargo screen, pale with contained anger.

"They are using my stolen waveform in the header. Not enough for identity load. Enough for scent."

Grim isolated the sound. The dead platform's trick, made theatrical. Not enough to unlock her. Enough to scent her. Enough for Black Hole pattern hunters, Aphex factions, and hungry believers to attach her to the myth without her consent.

"Can it trigger your lock?" Lars asked.

"Not directly. Too thin. But it scrapes the boundary. It wants associations to propagate."

"Dirty correlation window," Grim said.

"Dirty ethics," Mira replied.

Fabron closed the official notebook first. Then the paper one.

"Orders?" Lars asked.

Fabron stood. He looked angry in three jurisdictions.

"Aphex order: attend and prevent hostile monopoly. Clear Hand order: keep the authority narrow. Personal order: make sure nobody uses Dr. Sovan's voice again without consequence. All three are actionable."

"Coherence?"

Fabron did not know the old answer by memory. He answered anyway.

"Coherence pending."

Chapter 29: Black Hole Nursery

The nursery was hidden inside a hospital ship that had never treated a patient.

Its registry claimed disaster relief. The name on the hull was Mercy of Twelve Harbors, painted in blue letters beside a faded emblem of open hands. The docking lights blinked in the soft pattern medical ships used around frightened refugees. Its public beacon offered pediatric trauma care, epidemic isolation, and emergency organ synthesis. Everything about it was designed to make decent people open doors.

Havets found it behind a debris moon where the Syren parley route crossed a dead logistics lane. Fastulv stayed three light-minutes out, loud on civilian salvage bands and ready to become the visible mistake if anyone looked the wrong way. Fabron's Aphex team approached in a maintenance skiff wearing stolen sanitation credentials. Vigor's smugglers drifted closer under the thermal shadow of an ice hauler and complained over open comms about spoiled onions. Rauk's reactor dampers sat in two black cases under Lars's boots. They hummed with the particular menace of farm engineering used for war.

The hospital ship's outer lock smelled clean.

That was the first horror.

No rot. No gore in the corridors. No screaming. Just antiseptic air, polished grab rails, children's murals projected on bulkheads, and directional signs pointing toward TRIAGE, FAMILY REUNION, HYDRATION, COUNSELING. The walls were warm to the touch because the ship wanted bodies calm when they entered.

Below the public decks, past a locked personnel lift and three false quarantine seals, the cryo bays held no refugees.

They held growth racks, marrow vats, neural scaffolds, refrigerated blood, and failed interface bodies sorted by anomaly probability. Black Hole B-7 had learned to farm what it could not understand.

Lars lay in the observation duct above the main chamber and felt old anger arrive without the old neurological lock.

Not the old pain.

Memory with teeth.

Pluto cold. Heliostat hunger. Lyra's hands shaking over a body they could not save because the tool they needed had not been invented yet. The ancient argument that survival did not justify becoming the thing that hunted you. All of it came back clean enough to hurt properly.

Fabron lay beside him in infiltration armor, silent for once. His breathing was controlled through the helmet link. Too controlled. The main chamber below them reflected in his visor: rows of donor bays, transparent tubing, technicians in white suits with black wrist implants, and a central vat where something small floated in golden nutrient gel.

Grim occupied the ship's maintenance systems, but the usual dry confidence had thinned.

"Twenty-six living donors," Grim said. "Most ordinary by Aerit standards. Some resilient in ways Black Hole overvalues, as if durability were seasoning. One possible locked Aerit. Consent unavailable."

He paused.

"Additional category uncertain."

Lars knew what he meant. The central vat.

"Later," Lars said.

"Later may become a moral debt with interest."

"Everything in this room is debt."

There it was. The rule under pressure.

Fabron shifted enough for armor fabric to whisper. "If we do not wake them, they may die. That is not pressure. That is the operational fact."

"If we force it, we may kill who they are trying to remain."

"A hard line in a slaughterhouse."

"Yeah. That is why the boundary matters here."

Fabron looked down through the duct grille. A donor in Bay Fourteen twitched under sedation. A Black Hole technician adjusted the drip without looking at the face.

"I am responsible for every civilian I fail to extract," Fabron said.

"Yes."

"And for every one I turn into evidence by extracting badly."

"Also yes."

"Your doctrine is inconvenient."

"It's not doctrine today. It is an invasive step we do not take unless it helps the patient."

Mira fed structural weak points from L-Prime. She had refused to watch the visual feed until they needed her eyes. That refusal lasted eleven minutes. Then she opened one window, saw the vat labels, and went silent long enough for Lars to worry.

"I can help break the ship," she said finally. "Do not ask me to classify the people unless classification changes what you do."

"Understood."

"I mean that exactly. If the answer will not change a clamp, route, or drug, leave it out."

Grim said, "Boundary logged."

They moved.

Fabron's soldiers breached under a sanitation alert Grim had upgraded into a biohazard panic. The ship believed in its own cover enough to obey the wrong emergency. Doors sealed outward. Internal med drones rushed toward false contamination zones. Vigor's smugglers cut external clamps and attached rescue blisters to three maintenance ports. Rauk's reactor dampers made the power spine stutter like an old drunk, not failing, never failing, only missing rhythm enough to break central timing.

Lars entered through the ceiling.

He landed on a surgical track above the donor bays and did not use Ragnarrök. Not here. Not with vats, oxygen lines, and frightened bodies one bad shock away from dying. The first Black Hole surgeon wore human skin and insectile patience. Its white gloves were clean. Its badge said DR. ELLIAN VALE, PEDIATRIC RECONSTRUCTION. Its eyes looked at Lars as a variable.

It did not banter.

Neither did Lars.

He broke its arm, pinned it to a rack, and let Fabron foam it alive for interrogation. The foam hardened around its jaw before it could bite down on the implant behind its molars. Fabron's hands were efficient. His face was not.

"Alive," Fabron told his nearest corporal. "We need buyers, routes, command keys. If it dies without permission, I will be personally disappointed in everyone."

"Major," the corporal said, voice shaking, "yes."

A particulate swarm released toward the donor bays.

It came from the ceiling vents as a glitter so fine the eye wanted to call it dust. Grim's categories fractured across the channel: sampler, sterilizer, archive denial, tissue tagger, all at once. It was designed to decide what the room was while entering lungs.

Havets moved.

No visible miracle. No holy blue light. The swarm lost coordination at the level of timing. Particles argued with particles. A thousand tiny intentions missed the same beat. They fell like soot into containment fields that had not existed in the ship's logs five seconds earlier.

Grim made a sound Lars had never heard from him.

"Classification strain," Grim said. "These are not weapons only. They are hostile experiments with incomplete labels."

"Answer no," Lars said.

"Insufficient but emotionally satisfying."

The rescue became labor.

Not glorious labor. Awkward, slow, dangerous work. Fabron's soldiers cut restraints and transferred bodies into rescue blisters while med alarms lied at them. Vigor's smugglers kept count in kitchen shorthand because panic liked numbers it could hold: six warm, eight breathing assisted, two pressure unstable, one needs hands now. Rauk's dampers kept the ship from locking the bays. Mira found which tubes could be cut and which had to be capped, voice clipped to the bone.

"Bay Seven cap blue, cut green. Do not touch silver. Silver is spinal."

"Bay Nine has two feeds labeled nutrition. One is compliance drug. Cut the left by temperature, not color."

"Bay Twelve, wait."

Bay Twelve held Isaac Tam.

Broad-faced. Farmer's hands. A labor tattoo a century out of date on his inner wrist. His file called him forty-nine. His bone density said two hundred. Old fractures had healed too cleanly. His bloodwork carried impossible repair signatures under ordinary human noise. His neural lock patterns sang quiet refusal even under sedation.

Black Hole's file marked him: PROBABLE OLD-SOURCE HOST. OPENING RISK: HIGH. HARVEST PRIORITY: PRESERVE.

Lars wanted to tear the ship apart with his bare hands.

Instead, he breathed.

"Mira," he said, "do we move him without waking him?"

"Physical risk moderate. Cognitive risk lower if no identity language, no old greeting, no direct contradiction of cover memory. Keep evidence minimal. Ask for movement only."
